(SA1AN). Ms. Geraldine Burnett typed and produced the report.

- 111 -
PAKISTAN
BALOCHISTAN COMMUNITY IRRIGATION AND AGRICULTURE PROJECT
Credit and Project Summary
Borrower:           Islamic Republic of Pakistan
Beneficiaries:       Balochistan Irrigation and Agriculture Departments
Amount:              Credit: SDR 18.5 million (US$26.7 million equivalent)
Terms:               Credit: Standard, with 35 years maturity
Project Description:  The project initiates implementation of the Governments of Pakistan and
Balochistan's new irrigation and drainage strategy for developing efficient,
sustainable minor irrigation. In doing this, it undertakes interrelated actions to
greatly increase community involvement in project development, financing and
operation so as to increase beneficiaries' ownership and commitinent to project
works, and lead to the efficient and sustainable use of resources. These actions
are in three broad areas, that is, developing and strengthening community
organizations so they can assist plan and construct minor irrigation systems and
assume full responsibility for operating them; constructing appropriate irrigation
infrastructure which beneficiaries would be prepared to properly operate and
maintain; and changing and strengthening the Balochistan Irrigation Department's
capabilities so it can assist communities to eventually take a leading role in
developing their minor irrigation resources. The project is the second Bank
Group-assisted operation directed at developing Balochistan's minor irrigation.
Benefits:            Quantifiable benefits include increasing agricultural production on some 4,800
ha, and thus farm incomes for some 7,200 farm families, many of whom are
below the poverty line. Non-quantifiable benefits include: (a) developing strong
formal farmer organizations which could assist communities undertake other
development initiatives, and give communities a stronger voice in requesting
government services or organizing better agricultural input supply and commodity
marketing services; (b) improvements in health from the potable water supplies;
(c) some improvement in the status of rural women from their role in the
development of potable water supplies and the agricultural training they will
receive; and (d) improvements in village infrastructure resulting from local
communities investing beneficiaries repayments of irrigation infrastructure capital
costs in future village development.



- iv -
Risks:               Project implementation could be impaired by ineffective, unsustainable FOs,
tribal disputes, weak contractors and a too diffuse geographical spread of
schemes. As well, beneficiaries may not repay their share of scheme capital
costs. To counteract these risks the project would: (a) build FOs on traditional
village organizations, institute linkages with traditional village leaders and involve
them with FO leadership, train FO office-bearers in organizational management
and try to ensure FO members to have sufficient, appropriate incentives to join
and stay in FO; (b) screen out schemes where potential participants are involved
in intractable disputes, but also provide transparent methods for dispute
resolving, based on traditional dispute solving mechanisms; (c) under BMIAD,
PMU provided informal contractor training, and there is now a small group of
fairly proficient contractors with experience of minor irrigation construction.
Nevertheless, PMU must adhere to strict post-qualification review and selection
procedures, and rigorously monitor contract implementation; (d) to minimize
logistical difficulties, establish field offices so implementation staff are in
reasonably close contact with project operations, and when selecting schemes
give preference to those close to each other thus establishing clusters of schemes
within the various areas; and (e) beneficiaries would pay around 50% of their
share of capital costs up-front, that is, during design and construction. Scheme
construction would not be completed until all these funds were collected. FOs
would collect the remaining balances and deposit them into FO development
accounts in local banks. In agreement with the government administration, local
communities would use these funds for high priority local development
expenditures. These funds would remain in the local community and help
develop it, thus giving farmers an incentive to repay. Finally, GOBal would not
transfer ownership of any newly constructed infrastructure to FOs until their
members had fully contributed their share of the capital costs.
Poverty Category:    Program of Targeted Interventions. The project is included in the program of
targeted interventions for poverty alleviation because the proportion of the poor
(43%) among Balochistan's rural population, that is, project beneficiaries, is
significantly larger than their proportion (32%) in Pakistan's total population.
The project would increase incomes of small farmers (average irrigated area 0.5
ha versus an overall average 3-4 ha irrigated area for all Balochistan farms), and
provide additional wage employment for community members.
Economic Rate of Return: 14%
Financial Rate of Return: Depending on the cropping system, estimated financial rates of return for
farmers vary from 34 to 99%.

PAKISTAN
BALOCHISTAN COMMUNITY IRRIGATION AND AGRICULTURE PROJECT
I. PROVINCE AND SECTOR BACKGROUND
A. Introduction
1.1        The Provincial Government of Balochistan (GOBal) through the Government of Pakistan
(GOP) has requested International Development Association (IDA) assistance to further develop its
community minor irrigation programs. This project focuses strongly on developing sustainable farmer
organizations which can assist plan and construct minor irrigation systems, and assume full responsibility
for operating them. It is the second IDA-assisted project directed at developing Balochistan's minor
irrigation. The first, the Balochistan Minor Irrigation and Agricultural Development Project (Credit
1243-PAK) (BMIAD), commenced operations in late 1982, with the IDA credit closing some 11 years
later in December 1993. Costing around US$48.0 million equivalent, it developed 42 perennial irrigation
schemes with a command area of about 11,000 ha. Kreditanstalt fur Wiederaufbau (KfW) and the
Government of the Netherlands (GON) cofinanced the project. This project would develop and involve
farmer organizations (FOs) in planning, constructing and operating minor irrigation schemes; construct
minor, mini and flood irrigation systems plus potable water supplies where needed; provide
organizational, engineering and agricultural services to farmers; and strengthen the Balochistan Irrigation
Department (BID) capabilities to assist local communities develop minor irrigation.
1.2        GOBal, in collaboration with the cofinanciers of BMIAD and with assistance from the
BMIAD technical assistance team, identified and prepared the project. The project builds on the
implementation experiences of BMIAD, particularly regarding involving local communities in scheme
selection, design and operation. This staff appraisal report is based on the preparation report (Balochistan
Minor Irrigation and Agricultural Development Project Phase II Preparation Report, June 1993) and the
findings of pre-appraisal and appraisal missions which visited Balochistan during November 1993 and
April/May 1994 respectively.
B. Balochistan and Agricultural Sector
Physical Setting
1.3        Balochistan is the largest of Pakistan's four provinces (about 350,000 km2 or 44% of the
country's area), but is the least developed and has the smallest population (about seven million or 5.8%).
It lies north of the tropics, between the latitudes of 240 and 320, and between the longitudes of 600 to 700
east. To the north the province is bordered by Afghanistan and North West Frontier province (NWFP),
to the south by the Arabian sea, and to the west and east by Iran and Punjab and Sindh provinces
respectively. Topographically Balochistan is the eastern extension of the Iranian plateau, subdivided by
moderately high (1,000-2,000 m) to high (2,000-2,500 m) mountain ranges of limited accessibility which
drop to the Indus plain in the east and coastal plains in the south. The highest mountain, Loe Sar,
reaches 3,500 m. Generally, Balochistan's relief is 52% high and low mountains, 22% gravelly fans and
terraces, and 26% plains.
1.4        Balochistan, with its mountainous highlands, arid rangelands and desert lowlands, is the
western boundary to Pakistan's heartland, the Indus valley, where Pakistan's population, agriculture and



- 2 -
industry are concentrated. The Indus valley provides the markets for Balochistan's main production (coal,
gas, mutton, hides, fruit and vegetables), and supplies Balochistan needs for food, grain (wheat and rice)
and manufactured goods.
1.5         Balochistan is fundamentally an extremely dry area with water being the limiting factor in
development. Situated in the world's desert belt, its climatic conditions range from dry to hyper-arid.
Annual precipitation varies from less than 50 mm in the west to 400 mm in the northeast fringe adjoining
Punjab province. Rainfall is erratic and three to five years in ten are poor rainfall years. It generally
falls in two seasons, in the summer from June to September (low-altitudinal areas and lowland deserts)
and in the winter from October to May (mid- to high-altitudinal areas and high mountain slopes).
Temperatures vary widely from cool-temperate highland in the north to tropical lowland in the south.
Maximum summer temperatures in June and July may range from 38C in Quetta to over 500C in Sibi,
while minimum temperatures from December to February may range from chilling to sub-zero. In the
hotter areas annual evapotranspiration often exceeds 2,000 mm and overall crop production is not possible
without some form of irrigation.
1.6         Although Balochistan's land area is vast, much of it is desert and low grade rangeland due
to its arid climate and overgrazing. Mountain areas have sparse or no soil coverage and are surrounded
by outwash (gravelly) fans made by torrents rushing down normally dry river channels. Except in certain
areas, for example, piedmont and river plains, the soils are mostly thin and calcareous. Generally soils
have a high proportion of sand and gravel making them free draining except in some lowland plain areas
where clay fractions are higher and waterlogging and salinity problems may occur. Soils are generally
low in organic matter and prone to erosion, but respond well to organic and inorganic fertilizers.
Agricultural Sector
1.7         Agriculture is the mainstay of Balochistan's economy, accounting for some 60%  of
provincial gross domestic product, and employing around 67 % of the labor force. Crops contribute about
62% of commodity gross farm income and livestock 38%. While the major crops by area are wheat,
rice, sorghum, fruits, vegetables and oilseeds, total commodity gross farm income consists mainly of
vegetable production 24% (eight year average, 1983/84 - 1990/91), fruit 19%, milk 16%, meat 12%,
wheat 11 % and rice 8%.
1.8         Of Balochistan's 35 million ha, farmers cultivate only about 1.5 million ha or 4% of which
37 % is perennially irrigated and 63 % is partially-irrigated. Farmers grow partially-irrigated crops using
water harvesting/rainfall run-off or flash flood water diversion techniques known as khushkaba or sailaba
cultivation respectively. Of the remaining area, some 1.1 million ha (3%) is classified as forests, 21.0
million ha (60%) as rangeland, and 11.4 million ha (33%) as wasteland or not suitable for cultivation
(desert, mountainous, towns and cities).
1.9         Balochistan farmers generally can be split between those who grow rainfed crops (khushkaba
and sailaba) and also run small livestock flocks, and those who have access to irrigation water and grow
irrigated crops. The main rainfed crops are wheat, sorghum, rapeseed, mustard and fodder. The main
irrigated crops are wheat, rice, apples, apricots, peaches, grapes, pomegranate, dates and vegetables.
1.10        During the 1980s Balochistan's agricultural sector grew strongly. While growth rates of
provincial gross domestic product are not available, during the seven years 1983/84 to 1990/91



commodity gross farm income in real terms grew at a little more than 9% annually. In comparison total
Pakistan real commodity gross farm income grew at about 4.8% annually. Increased fruit and livestock
production mainly contributed to Balochistan's good performance.
1.11        Perennial Irrigation. Balochistan's perennial irrigation consists of two general operational
systems. The first is large gravity-fed canal systems supplied from the Indus river, that is, the Pat
Feeder, Kirther and Desert canals which command some 312,700 ha and annually irrigate about 234,000
ha. The second is small or minor irrigation systems, which includes both small schemes (50 to 400 ha)
supplied from karezes (tunnel or underground channel tapping an aquifer), or from infiltration galleries
or small weirs in rivers, and individuals pumping from tubewells or open wells. Minor and individual
systems irrigate about 320,000 ha per year, while in total perennial systems irrigate about 0.55 million
ha annually. While minor and individual irrigation systems have grown considerably over the last
decade, the efficiencies of water resource development and water delivery to the farm are often poor.
C. Issues, Objectives and Strategies
Issues
1.12        Social, technical, policy and institutional issues constrain the efficient, sustainable and
equitable development and use of Balochistan's minor irrigation resources. These issues include tribal
societies and the problems caused by their many disputes concerning water and land rights, the scattered
and small nature of water sources, inappropriate capital cost recovery policies, outmoded development
policies which do not sufficiently involve beneficiaries in the planning and implementation of schemes,
inequitable distribution of existing water rights at some water sources, and weak agricultural extension
services.
1.13        Water Resource Development. Because Balochistan is so dry, irrigators have developed
practically all water sources. Often, however, this development has been quite inefficient, with the source
not being fully developed and or large water delivery losses occurring between the source and the
farmgate.  Contributing to this are the difficulties of organizing the tribal societies who inhabit
Balochistan's rural areas, plus tribal disputes concerning water and land rights; no sources of long-term
development capital except for individual savings; weak technical services of the Balochistan Irrigation
Department (BID); and weak construction contractors. The tribal groups normally do not allow large
outside contractors into their areas, and thus only small, local contractors, who have little equipment and
technical expertise, can compete for work.
1.14        Water Sources. Minor irrigation's perennial water sources consist of streams, karezes,
wells and tubewells. Because of the province's aridity and topography, these sources are small and
scattered. Most sources supply only two to four cusecs. This mitigates against economies of scale in
construction, infrastructure and supply of services.
1.15        Capital Cost Recovery. GOBal does not recover any of its public investments in minor
irrigation infrastructure. This sends the wrong signals to irrigators concerning capital investment costs
and economic returns from irrigation, reduces their "ownership attitude" towards a scheme, reduces
public funds available for investment, and is inequitable unless targeted towards poverty alleviation. Until
the advent of BMIAD, GOBal recovered little or none of the public funds expended on minor irrigation



operation and maintenance (O&M). Under BMIAD, scheme beneficiaries are providing cash and labor
to meet 100% of O&M expenditures (see para 1.27).
1.16       Beneficiary Participation. In the past BID planned, implemented and operated government-
funded minor irrigation systems in a "top-down" manner. Rarely did BID engineers meet with irrigators
to learn how they wanted the scheme designed and operated, or who held water and land rights and
whether the community was split by disputes over these rights. BID did not attempt to develop local
organizations who could sustain the operation of the scheme. Under BMIAD, BID has developed good
methods for screening and selecting potentially successful minor irrigation schemes. BID now collects
appropriate data on water and land rights, community disputes, community land and water resources,
agricultural production and community income and health. BID also takes account of beneficiaries'
comments on scheme layout. BID, however, should involve beneficiaries more in scheme planning and
construction. BID must promote the development of viable, sustainable FOs who eventually would be
responsible for constructing and operating their own minor irrigation schemes. BID should become more
a supplier of technical services to such FOs, rather than a constructor of schemes.
1.17       Water Rights. Water, not land, is usually the limiting factor to agricultural development
in minor irrigation areas. In some instances one or a few families hold the major portion of water rights.
GOBal, by developing such water sources, increased inequitable income distribution within the
community. When selecting schemes to develop, GOBal must pay particular attention to water rights
being reasonably equitably distributed so that a small number of beneficiaries do not receive the major
share of scheme benefits (see para 1.27).
1.18       Weak Agricultural Extension Services. Because of the high per unit costs of developing
minor irrigation, beneficiaries generally need to change their cropping patterns to higher value crops to
obtain attractive returns and maximize benefits. This is particularly so at mid- and low-altitudinal sites
where farmers are mainly growing low value grain crops. Balochistan Agricultural Department's (DOA)
extension services are generally weak and poorly equipped to assist farmers make such cropping changes.
Its field staff are mainly inadequately trained with insufficient financial resources. While the Balochistan
Agricultural Extension and Adaptive Research Project (Credit 1533-PAK) (BAEAR) better organized the
service and provided training, technical assistance, equipment and operating funds, DOA implemented
it in only four districts (Loralai, Nasirabad, Kachhi and Lasbela), and so far the service has been only
mildly successful in encouraging farmers to adopt improved, more remunitative technology.
Objectives
1.19       As for GOP, the primary objectives of Balochistan's medium-term development strategy are
to accelerate efforts to tackle poverty and human resources development issues; strengthen the enabling
environment for the private sector; and improve the management of natural resources and the
environment. In agriculture GOBal's objectives are to increase output growth and raise per capita
incomes in an efficient and sustainable manner with particular emphasis on enhancing productivity and
improving natural resource management. Sustaining a high rate of agricultural growth is important for
poverty alleviation. As well, agriculture has to be environmentally sustainable.



5-
Strategies
1.20        As part of its overall strategy for economic development and structural adjustment, in the
agricultural sector GOP/GOBal intend to: (a) promote the private sector's role in all markets including
irrigation and rural infrastructure; (b) address policy distortions by reducing subsidies for credit, water
and fertilizers, and freeing prices and trade in other inputs and commodities; (c) strengthen the
management of public expenditures with better prioritization to ensure effective delivery of essential
services; and (d) rationalize the budget process in the sector by ensuring funding of sectoral priorities
including ongoing and new investment projects.
Bank Strategy
1.21        The Bank generally supports GOBal's agricultural sector development objectives and
strategies. The Bank agrees with the maintaining of a high rate of agricultural growth (at least 4% per
annum), with emphasis on further improving the link between agricultural growth and poverty alleviation,
and ensuring environrmentally sustainable agricultural development. The Bank's short- and medium-term
assistance will focus on: (a) redefining the roles of the public and private sectors in all aspects of
agriculture with fundamental shifts in the irrigation and drainage subsector towards more
commercialization of the system; (b) reducing distortions in input and output prices and trade regimes;
(c) improving the efficiency of agricultural public expenditures; (d) strengthening natural resource
management by emphasizing local institutions; and (e) strengthening support services to increase
productivity and diversify agriculture.
1.22        The Bank, with assistance from the Water and Power Development Authority of Pakistan
(WAPDA), has formulated a new long-term irrigation and drainage strategy for Pakistan (Pakistan:
Irrigation and Drainage Issues and Options, March 25, 1994). GOP generally agrees to this strategy.
The strategy is based on the Bank Group's water resource management policy (Water Resources
Management: A World Bank Policy Paper, 1993), and promotes the use of market-determined incentives
for improving the supply and management of irrigation and drainage services. It would: (a) redefine the
role of government and commercialize and later privatize any water service that is not a public good; (b)
develop water markets by instituting enforceable property rights to water and legalizing water markets;
(c) develop autonomous, commercially-oriented public utilities on a canal command basis to sell water
to FOs, covering all their current O&M costs and future investment expenditures; (d) develop regulatory
bodies to ensure the utilities do not create monopoly profits; and (e) develop and strengthen FOs which
initially would organize O&M of the farm-to-farm supply canal, ensure water is distributed in accordance
with property rights, monitor groundwater use, organize on-farm drainage development and collect water
delivery and drainage charges. FOs could eventually control and operate a larger part of the irrigation
distributory system, that is, as well as the watercourse (the farm-to-farm distributory system), part of or
all of the tertiary, secondary and main canal distributory system.
1.23        With regard to Balochistan's minor irrigation systems, the Bank's new policy would
emphasize: (a) redefining the role of government; (b) further developing nascent water markets;
(c) instituting legislation and regulations plus any required regulatory body to monitor and advise on the
sustainable use of available irrigation water resources; and (d) organizing/developing FOs which are
capable of constructing, controlling and operating sustainable minor irrigation systems. As under tribal
or customary law, minor system irrigators already have individual water rights, and markets for water
rights do exist, the further development of water markets will be easier than in Indus basin systems.



- 6 -
Additionally, the minor irrigation systems are so small they do not require the development of public
utilities to sell water to FOs.
D. Bank Group and Other Support for Irrigation and Lessons Learned
Donor Support
1.24       Many bilateral and multilateral international agencies and donors are supporting irrigation
and agricultural development in Balochistan. Amongst the more relevant and active are: (a) Kuwait Fund
for Arab Economic Development which assisted to finance a Small Irrigation Schemes in Balochistan
Project. The project constructed or improved 39 small irrigation schemes covering about 48,000 ha; (b)
Overseas Development Administration (ODA) of the United Kingdom also financed a Small Irrigation
Schemes in Balochistan Project which constructed/improved seven small irrigation schemes; (c) Asian
Development Bank (ADB) which is assisting to finance: (i) Balochistan Groundwater and Trickle
Irrigation Project aimed at improving the efficiency of groundwater development and water delivery to
crops; (ii) Pakistan Fruit and Vegetable Marketing Project directed at establishing organized fruit and
vegetable markets in the provinces (see para 5.4); (iii) with the Overseas Economic Cooperation Fund
of Japan (OECF) as cofinancier, the Pat Feeder Rehabilitation Project directed at rehabilitating and
expanding the Pat Feeder canal system; and (iv) with the United States Agency for International
Development (USAID), the Pakistan Flood Control Project which is undertaking flood protection studies
and works throughout Pakistan including a study of managing flood irrigation systems; (d) USAID which
financed the Balochistan Area Development Project. The project covered several development activities
including construction of delay action dams to recharge aquifers and rehabilitation of karezes; (e) United
Nations Development Program (UNDP) and Food and Agricultural Organization of the United Nations
(FAO) is funding and implementing the Fruit Development in Balochistan Project (third phase). This
project is aimed at improving the productivity of and income from irrigated deciduous fruit orchards.
It has established a Deciduous Fruit Development Center which undertakes research and training in plant
protection, nutrition, irrigation, storage, cultivation techniques, varieties, marketing and economics of
deciduous fruit production. UNDP, with the World Bank as executing agency, also funded the Water
Sector Investment Planning Study, 1988, which reviewed the effectiveness of present irrigation projects
throughout Pakistan, assessed the ability of provincial irrigation departments to plan and construct
projects, and prepared a ten year irrigation and drainage investment program which prioritized projects
in the pipeline. It also established a planning cell for irrigation within Balochistan's Planning and
Development Department (P&D).
1.25       Non-Govenment Organizations. Non-governmental organizations (NGOs) are slowly
becoming involved in the management of natural resources, including irrigation development, in
Balochistan. While there are no large NGOs organizing local communities to develop and operate
irrigation schemes such as the Aga Khan Rural Support Program (AKRSP) at Gilgit in northern Pakistan,
several smaller NGOs recently became active. Of these the most important is the Balochistan Rural
Support Program (BRSP) which operates in some 200 villages, and assists to organize formal and
informal groups to implement community development schemes, including irrigation.
Bank Group Support
1.26       Over the recent past the Bank Group has generally directed its project assistance in the
Balochistan agricultural sector towards improving the productivity and sustainability of agricultural



- 7 -
infrastructure and resource use. These projects may be subdivided into projects to: (a) rehabilitate
existing irrigation systems and improve the efficiency of water delivery to the farm; (b) improve and
expand minor irrigation; (c) improve agricultural research and extension institutions and services; and
(d) improve protection of the environment and management of natural resources. These projects include
respectively: (a) Irrigation Systems Rehabilitation I and II (Cr. 1239-PAK) and (Cr. 1888-PAK),
Command Water Management (Cr. 1487-PAK) and On-Farm Water Management 1, II and III (Cr. 1163-
PAK), (Cr. 1603-PAK) and (Cr. 2245/Ln. 3327-PAK); (b) BMIAD; (c) Agricultural Research I and H
(Cr. 1158-PAK) and (Cr. 2154-PAK), and BAEAR; and (d) Balochistan Natural Resource Management
(Cr. 2584-PAK) (BNRM). The Bank has also been the executing agency for a series of projects to
provide rural jobs and repair environmental and natural resource damage done by Afghan refugees, that
is, the Income Generating Project for Refugee Areas I, II and III.
Lessons Learned
1.27       BMIAD, the first Bank Group-assisted project specifically directed at improving
Balochistan's minor irrigation, commenced in 1982. Although the IDA credit closed December 1993,
GOBal expects to complete the project around mid-1995. The project's primary objectives were to
increase agricultural production and farm incomes by expanding minor irrigation and improving
agricultural practices, and to develop GOBal's capacity to plan and implement minor irrigation
development with appropriate local community participation. The project had considerable early
difficulties including: (a) social constraints as the project paid insufficient attention to the complex water
and land rights pertaining to tribal areas and to scheme designs acceptable to the local communities;
(b) remoteness of the sites which were widely dispersed with often poor access; (c) weak contractors as
tribal influence only allows small inexperienced local contractors to bid for work, plus difficulties
associated with remoteness of the sites; and (d) poor organizational and management arrangements which
included a federal as well as provincial agencies. GOBal has generally overcome the social and
management constraints. It handled social issues by developing a comprehensive screening of schemes
before accepting them for construction, and by involving beneficiaries in scheme planning and design.
Major screening criteria for scheme selection include: (a) no major unsolved disputes in area; (b) top
quartile of water right holders should not control more than 70% of total water rights; (c) top quartile
of land owners should not own more than 70% of total available land; (d) schemes must have potential
for increasing agricultural production; and (e) schemes must have a 10% or higher estimated economic
rate of return (LRR). It improved project organization and management by reorganizing the Project
Management Cell within BID responsible for implementing the project, including giving the Project
Director procurement authority for up to Rs 20.0 million, and through a large infusion of local and
international technical assistance (financed by GON). This provided both expert advice and additional
staff capability, particularly for designing the infrastructure works. The schemes were usually designed
to minimize O&M costs which sometimes considerably increased capital costs. Additionally, GOBal has
handed over O&M responsibilities to scheme beneficiaries at all sites, and beneficiaries are operating the
schemes and providing labor and/or cash for maintenance. To date 45 out of a total 47 water user
associations have opened O&M bank accounts in which they currently hold an average Rs 9,000, while
all associations have organized to supply O&M labor.
1.28       Major lessons learned from BMIAD and similar projects elsewhere include: (a) schemes
must be carefully screened according to social as well as technical and economic criteria; (b) to ensure
ownership of schemes all beneficiaries/stakeholders must be effectively involved in project planning and
implementation; (c) with nominal outside inputs (concrete-makers, trolleys and so on) local communities



- 8 -
can construct simple engineering works at considerably less cost than local contractors, and local
contractors need to be screened and monitored; (d) irrigation works require an adequate aftercare period
to ensure they operate as expected; (e) project implementation requires a strong unified multi-disciplinary
management team; (f) NGOs can help interface between local communities and government agencies; (g)
schemes should be generally clustered to reduce administration overheads; and (h) project staff should
continually monitor the progress of community/natural resource development projects and periodically
evaluate their results and impact, making any necessary program adjustments.
1.29       Consequently the project is designed to: (a) carefully screen all schemes according to social,
economic and technical criteria; (b) involve beneficiaries in scheme planning and implementation, and
identify/organize/strengthen FOs from scheme screening onwards; (c) ensure that in selected schemes FOs
fully understand their obligations and agree to contribute towards scheme capital costs and be fully
responsible for operating and maintaining the schemes; (d) consider and evaluate all technical options
when designing schemes; (e) provide an approximately two-year aftercare period following the
construction of each scheme's main irrigation works, plus initiating changes in BID so it can better serve
mninor irrigators; (f) undertake appropriate qualification and monitoring procedures concerning
contractors; (g) cluster schemes in about four general locations; (h) ensure strong project management
which adopts a multi-disciplinary approach to scheme development; and (i) continually monitor project
implementation progress, and periodically evaluate results and impacts and make any necessary program
adjustments.
1.30       Future Direction. Following its new irrigation and drainage policy, the Bank Group will
encourage GOBal to have BID eventually become a supplier of technical services to minor irrigation FOs
rather than a constructor of schemes. In the long-term this implies GOBal would reorganize BID
(perhaps to become part of a Provincial Water Authority) so it can administer irrigation in Balochistan
at the least cost to GOBal and provide the greatest returns to irrigators. The organization would provide
technical services at cost and schemes would be self-funding regarding O&M and future replacements and
development. In the long-term, perhaps in 10 to 15 years, Balochistan's irrigation administration could
consist of a Balochistan Provincial Water Authority responsible for policy on all provincial water issues;
public utilities in the Indus-supplied areas retailing water to FOs; a Directorate of Community Irrigation
coordinating and administering community schemes and FOs owning, operating, maintaining, and further
developing the schemes.  However, restructuring of the irrigation industry cannot be achieved
immediately, and must be done in steps. In the immediate future, BID needs to reorient its work
programs in minor irrigation and bring local conmnunities fully into the planning, implementation and
operation of minor irrigation development. This would allow FOs/beneficiaries in the next five to six
years to take responsibility for all aspects of future scheme developments, meeting not only 100% of
O&M costs but also most capital development expenditures. BID and DOA should rethink the services
they presently can supply minor irrigators, and make them more appropriate, for example, have
appropriately trained staff who can interact and organize/strengthen/train FOs in administration, finance
handling and other aspects. NGOs and the private sector could also be involved. GOBal must legislate
a more appropriate legal structure for FOs which are presently being organized under the Water User
Association regulations. These are unsuitable for minor irrigation FOs which have wider aims and
objectives. This project will accelerate Balochistan's minor irrigation development in these new
directions, and is an important component in GOP, GOBal and the Bank Groups' programs to increase
irrigation productivity and sustainability, and to improve natural resource management.



- 9 -
E. Rationale for Bank Group Involvement
1.31        Country Assistance Strategy. The World Bank Group's country assistance strategy for
Pakistan focusses on two closely linked development goals: poverty reduction and reducing constraints
to sustained growth. To address the poverty reduction objective the Bank Group's approach highlights
improving inadequate basic social services and so improving weak social indicators. To support the
growth objective, which will help with the income-related aspects of poverty reduction, Bank Group
efforts concentrate on: structural reforms, with an emphasis on public finance issues and improving the
enabling environment for the private sector; strengthening inadequate basic infrastructure; reducing
constraints on agriculture productivity; and protecting Pakistan's natural resources base. This project fits
into the Bank Group's country assistance strategy as it would implement the Bank's new irrigation
strategy for Pakistan, allowing the private sector to play a larger role in irrigation development and
operation, thus improving productivity in these areas, increase agricultural production and farm incomes,
and improve the management and sustainability of irrigation water resources. As project beneficiaries
have a larger incidence of poverty than Pakistan overall, by raising farm incomes the project will do
much to alleviate poverty.
1.32        Rationale for IDA Involvement. IDA's role is crucial to achieving the project objectives,
specifically to redefine the role of governmnent, develop FOs capable of assisting to construct and to
operate the irrigation systems, and rationalize the use of public investment funds in irrigation
development. IDA is well placed to assist GOBal with policy issues that relate to water resources
development and management in Balochistan's environment with inland drainage basins. IDA has a
comparative advantage relative to other donors in these areas, and can also contribute considerably to
encouraging coordination between donors on key policy and institutional issues. The project would also
complement BMIAD.
II. PROJECT AREA
A. Location and Climate
Location
2.1         The project area covers all of Balochistan, however, available water resources and scheme
selection criteria are expected to limit project sites to mainly the northern and central mountains and
rangelands. Further, to minimize administrative costs, the project would try to generally cluster schemes
in four areas, that is, around or near the towns of Zhob, Loralai, Sibi and Khuzdar. These areas are at
altitudes ranging from below 1,000 m to above 2,000 m.
Climate
2.2         Rainfall. As mentioned, Balochistan's rainfall is low and erratic. The higher and more
effective rainfall occurs at higher altitudes where evaporation is lower. Average annual rainfalls at
possible project sites ranges from around 150 mmn near Sibi, 200-250 mm near Khuzdar and Loralai, to
300-350 mm near Zhob. In higher areas these rainfall figures are most probably understated as
meteorological stations are located on valley floors which usually receive less rainfall than the
surrounding mountains. Most project sites have a predominantly winter rainfall, which is gentler and



- 10 -
more effective than summer rainfall.  However, the lower areas in the province's east receive
predominantly summer rainfall.
2.3        Temperature. Altitude mainly determines the temperature regime in Balochistan. This
varies from cool-temperate to tropical, and is an asset giving Balochistan a comparative advantage for
growing a large variety of crops. The cool-temperate temperature zone is associated with the high
altitude belt (higher than 2,000 m) which has cold, freezing winters and cool summers. Indicator plants
for this area include conifers, plums, cherries and high quality apples. The temperate zone coincides with
the mid-altitude belt (1,300-2,000 m) which has cold winters with frosts and snow common, and mild
summers with temperatures rarely over 350 C. Indicator plants include wild pistachio and ash, temperate
fruits such as apple, peach and apricot, and vegetables, wheat and barley. The continental or inland low
altitudinal belt and lowlands (less than 1,300 m) have a subtropical climate where plant indicators are
acacias, prosopic, date palm, pomegranate, citrus and inter-row grain and feed crops. Winters are mild
to warm while summers are extremely hot with mid-day temperatures exceeding 50 C on the Kachhi
plan. Tropical temperatures prevail in the low altitudinal belt and lowlands facing the Arabian sea. This
zone is indicated by Acacia senegal, Maerua, Commiphora mukal and tropical fruits such as coconut
palm, papaya and mango. Winters are mild, and the Arabian sea moderates summer temperatures.
B. Land Systems and Soils
Land Systems
2.4        Balochistan can be divided into ten land systems!' delineated by land form, altitude and
indicator plants, that is, relief, climate and vegetation. The most relevant of these for this project are:
(a)   northern Sulaiman mountain range and rangelands (1.2 million ha) with altitudes
from 1,300 to 3,500 m and a summer rainfall (dry to semi-arid).Y' This is an
important orchard and natural rangeland area;
(b)   Brahui-Tobar-Kakar northern and central mountains and rangelands (11.6 million ha)
with altitudes from 900 to 3,500 m and a winter rainfall (dry to arid). This is the
main upland orchard area, and generally is the most productive natural rangeland;
(c)   Marri-Bugti hills (5.3 million ha) with altitudes from 300 to 1,300 m and a mainly
summer rainfall (semi-arid to arid). This is a natural rangeland and khushkaba
wheat, sorghum and millet growing area;
Ministry of Foreign Affairs, Government of the Netherlands, Dr. Hein
van Gils and M. Shabbir Baig, editors, Bnviroxjmental Profile,
Balochistan,  Pakistan,  Wolters-Noordhoff,  Groningen,  Netherlands,
January 1992.
2/         Rainfall  classification  is: dry 250-400  mm/yr;  semi-arid  200-250
mm/yr; arid 150-200 mm/yr; and hyper-arid 0-100 mm/yr.



- I1 -
(d)   piedmont plains around Sibi, Kachhi and Nasirabad (2.2 million ha) with altitudes
from 150 to 200 m and a summer rainfall. This is Balochistan's main grain
producing area and is irrigated by Indus river water; and
(e)   northern Pab-Kirthar mountain ranges (1.7 million ha) with altitudes from 900 to
1,600 m and a summer rainfall (semi-arid).  This is a natural rangeland and
khushkaba wheat and sorghum area.
Soils
2.5         Soils in Balochistan are generally calcareous (5 to 30% lime), mostly alkaline (pH 7.8 to
8.2), and extremely low in organic matter with a low water holding capacity (15 to 19%). Depending
on water availability, more than half of the soils are suitable for agriculture. The mountain slopes are
mostly bare or thinly covered with coarse, highly calcareous material. Soil depth in the valleys is
generally adequate, and lime content quite low. Piedmont plain soils are deep, well drained, fine
textured, and calcareous with an 18 to 20% lime content uniformly distributed in the profile. Soils are
generally easily worked, but are prone to capping. Occasionally localized poor drainage occurs because
of a hard pan due to repeated shallow cultivation.
C. Water Resources and Irrigation
Water Resources
2.6        Surface Water. Balochistan has limited water resources. Balochistan's share of water from
the Indus river, according to the Water Accord of March 1991, is about 3.87 million acre feet (MAF).
In addition to the total allocated, 12% of water captured from floods and new reservoirs is allocated to
Balochistan. At present about 2.04 MAF of Indus water is being utilized in Balochistan from Guddu and
Sukkur barrages through the Pat Feeder, Desert and Kirther Canal systems.
2.7        In addition to the Indus basin approximately thirteen river basins can be identified
throughout Balochistan. These are mostly hill torrents and small streams (nullahs), flowing only after
intense rain storms. Perennial flows only exist from springs at the base of hill ranges or at locations
downstream where impermeable layers or changes in bed slope bring water above surface which
otherwise flows in sub-surface gravel. Estimated annual flows from these rivers amounts to about 3
MAF11. According to their drainage pattern, these rivers can be divided into four groups:
(a)   Kachhi Plain drainage - main rivers are the Nari, Sukleji, Mula, Chakkar, Lahri,
Chattr, Bolan, Karkh. Annual flows into this basin is about 0.89 MAF;
(b)  Indus system drainage - the Zhob river in the north-east has an annual flow of
about 0.25 MAF and the Gaj river in south about 0.12 MAF;
Surface Water Resources Development in Balochistan Province, Available
Land and Water Potential and its Utilization, Planning Directorate
(South), Planning and Investigation, WAPDA, May 1992.



- 12 -
(c)   Arabian Sea drainage - mainly the Dasht river basin, Hingol, Porali, Hub, Winder,
and small basins along the coast which have annual flows of about 1.55 MAF; and
(d)   Kharan Desert drainage - namely the Mashkel basin, Baddo and Morjen river
basins, and Pishin and Lora basins which have annual flows of about 0.28 MAF.
2.8        Groundwater. Groundwater underlying the wide river valleys is an important source of
water in the province. Groundwater quality is generally good in upland areas but it is poor in the coastal
strip and Kachhi plains. Total groundwater potential in the province is roughly estimated to range from
500 to 800 cusec. In 1990 the province had more than 10,000 tubewells, more than 3,000 dugwells and
about 800 functional karezes for irrigation. In certain areas, increasing exploitation by tubewells is
causing water tables to fall at the rate of about 0.3 meters per year. Both BNRM (through appropriate
aquifer access criteria and legislation) and the Power Sector Development Project (Ln. 3764-PAK)
(energy pricing policies) are addressing this problem.
Irrigation Systems
2.9        Depending upon the water source, the crop watering systems in Balochistan can be divided
into four types:
(a)   Perennial Indus canal systems. The largest perennial major canal systems are in
Nasirabad division deriving water from the Indus river as part of the Indus Basin
Irrigation system. These systems command an area of 312,700 ha and average
cropping intensity is about 75 percent. Drawing water from Guddu barrage, the Pat
Feeder canal commands an area of 185,500 ha. Other channels such as Uch,
Manuthy, Faizabad, Frontier, Khan Wah and direct outlets from the Desert canal
command an area of 52,700 ha. Taking water from Sukkur barrage, the Kirther
canal commands an area of 74,500 ha. About 2.04 MAF of water are diverted into
these systems. Like the rest of the Indus systems these are gravity flow systems with
a fixed rotation schedule (warabandi) at the watercourse level.
(b)   Minor perennial irrigation schemes. Minor irrigation includes both individual
irrigators and group-operated schemes with service areas from around 50 to 400 ha.
This project is only concerned with group-operated schemes. Individual irrigators
pump from open wells (irrigating some 10,000 ha) or tubewells (about 130,000 ha).
With the extension of the electrical network tubewells are increasing rapidly. Group-
operated schemes obtain their water supplies from karezes and perennial springs
(some 50,000 ha), and from infiltration galleries or weirs (about 130,000 ha). In
total, individuals irrigate approximately 140,000 ha, while small schemes irrigate
about 180,000 ha. Group-operated schemes often consist of a mixture of community
and government built structures which usually are inefficient in the capture and
transmission of irrigation supplies. Major losses usually occur in the main canal,
that is, in the transmission of irrigation water from the source or intake to the service
area, however, often the headworks or water intake also operate poorly. A typical
example is a damaged or silted-up weir. Some structures have reached the end of
their useful life, some are inadequately or poorly designed, some are not fully
completed and some are built using village or low technology. On many schemes,



- 13 -
through rehabilitation or new construction, there is considerable scope to increase
irrigation supplies.
(c)   Water harvesting (khushkaba). Rainfall runoff from a large area is concentrated
into a smaller area by bunding or where runoff is captured from small nullahs by
bunds and channeled to adjacent lands.
(d)   Flood irrigation (sailaba). In flood irrigation, flood water from non-perennial rivers
and hill torrents is diverted onto fields. A string of villages attempt to divert the
spate flow onto their fields by constructing breachable earth bunds across the river,
or by constructing stone/gravel spurs leading towards the center of the river.
Captured water flows from field to field and when the soil profile is saturated, the
lower bund is breached to release water into another field. Such irrigation is not
reliable because the flood flows are either too small and do not reach the command
area or too large to handle. Large flows in the beginning of the flood season wash
away the diversion spurs and it is not possible to re-build during that flood season.
Khushkaba and sailaba cultivation commands about 0.9 to 1.0 million ha. However,
the annual average cropping intensity is only around 20%, thus farmers grow about
200,000 ha of crops annually in this manner.
D. Population and Ethnic Groups
2.10        Balochistan's population is currently around 7.1 million, and is growing at least at the
national average of 3.1 % per year. Family planning programs so far have had little success, thus
Balochistan's population will continue to increase for many years to come with serious environmental
consequences. Because of its aridity, large proportion of people employed in agriculture and small
cultivable area, Balochistan's average population density is only 20 persons per km2 compared to the
national average of about 125.
2.11       Balochistan's population consists of three major groups whose identity defines their socio-
political and economic organization: the Balochi, Pathan and Brahui. Several ethnic minorities, such as
Sindhi, Punjabi, Lassi, Mongol Hazara and Muhajir, also inhabit the province. Each group is associated
with a particular territory. Ethnic minorities as well as larger groups each have their own distinct
language. The largest are the Balochi, who are spread out over the south-western Makran region,
western (Chagai, Kharan) and eastern areas (Marri-Bugti, Sibi, Kachhi). These areas fall within three
land systems: the Brahui-Tobar-Kakar central mountains and rangelands; the piedmont plains around Sibi
and Kachhi and the coastal plains. The Balochi (meaning "nomad" or "wanderer"), who include nomadic
tribes such as the Marri, are an ethno-linguistic group in that they have a common language and share
cultural characteristics but are ethnically diverse.
2.12       The second largest group, the Pathan, inhabit roughly the province's northern third in Zhob,
Loralai, Quetta and Pishin regions in the Brahui-Tobar-Kakar northern and central mountains and
rangelands. Pathans are an ethnic group of Afghan origin. Their tribes native to Balochistan include the
Kakar, Tarin, Pani and Shirani. The Brahui, the third largest group, mainly occupy the Kalat plateau
around Mastung, Kalat and Khuzdar towns, which make up the Brahui-Tobar-Kakar central mountain and
rangeland system. The Brahui, also an ethno-linguistic group, is a confederacy of indigenous Brahui



- 14 -
tribes, such as the Ahmadzai and Iltazai, that has incorporated from time to time a number of Jat, Pathan
and Balochi.
2.13       Important distinctions exist in socio-political organization among the three major groups.
Balochi and Brahui tribes are large, diverse, usually highly stratified entities composed of units of
separate genealogical origin, clustering around a head group known as the Sardar Khel. Pathan tribes
are less stratified, generally composed of a number of smaller kindred groups descended from a common
ancestor. Pathan leadership is not necessarily hereditary while the Balochi and Brahui are organized
under a hereditary chief and headmen. These social distinctions are expressed in economic organization,
especially in the rules governing land and water rights. Although there is a degree of variation within
each group, tradable land and water rights are more prevalent in Balochi and Brahui economic systems
than among the Pathan.
2.14       Vulnerable Groups. Vulnerable groups are defined as social groups with a cultural identity
distinct from the dominant society which makes them vulnerable to being marginalized in the economic
development process. In Pakistan, these groups comprise nomadic pastoralists and isolated groups living
in remote areas. The project will not detrimentally affect vulnerable groups, as defined. Those schemes
through which pastoralists migrate will not impact negatively on them, since these are minor schemes and
grazing lands will not be inundated. Some schemes may include remote mountain villages whose incomes
will be improved as a result of the project's equity criteria and development impact.
E. Farm Size, Property Rights, Land Use and Cropping Systems
Fann Size and Property Rights
2.15       Farm Size. While cropped land is usually privately owned in Balochistan, practically all
rangeland is tribally owned. Thus farm size only refers to the area privately owned and cropped, but not
to the area a farmer may have the right to graze. Farmers using khushkaba and sailaba cropping
techniques (low input) have considerably larger farms than those with perennial irrigation water. In much
of the land systems relevant to the project the average khushkaba/sailaba farm size is around 15 ha.
Generally, the smaller and more erratic rainfall is in an area, the larger the farms will be.
Khushkaba/sailaba farmers crop a varying percentage of their farm each year depending on rainfall, and
season to season variation can be large. Generally, sailaba sites predominate in summer rainfall areas.
Perennially irrigated farms supplied from minor irrigation sources tend to be small, and at the proposed
project sites already identified irrigated areas per farm average from about 0.2 to 1.9 ha (0.5 ha is the
overall expected average). In the higher areas farmers incur considerable costs to develop and protect
their irrigated areas. These average irrigated areas per farm are small when compared to an average
irrigated area of about 4.0 ha for farms in Balochistan's Indus supplied irrigation areas.
2.16       Land Tenure.!' Most of Balochistan's land is held under customary or tribal law. Formal
cadastral surveys with land titles being systematically registered have only been undertaken in areas
originally administered by the British. The Land Reform Act 1967 established a Record of Rights which
For an in-depth discussion of land tenure and water rights see Volume
2, Annex V, Poverty Study, of the project preparation report, that is,
Balochistan Minor Irrigation and Agricultural Development Project
Phase II, June 1993.



- 15 -
is a cadastral record for land and water rights registration. For land to be entered into the Record of
Rights, people from the concerned village or community walk the land's boundary, and agree to them.
This registration process gives security of tenure to an individual owner. Land registration is continuing,
but not on a systematic basis. Individual ownership is mainly confined to cultivated land. Tribal
authorities control and operate their tribal or communal lands, which are mainly rangelands. All tribal
members have a right to use their tribal land. Generally, when tribal members agree to cultivate tribal
land not previously cultivated, although the land is communally owned, tribal authorities allocate plots
to individuals. Over time these allocations may become permanent.
2.17       In the nine perennial irrigation schemes already screened, individuals own practically all the
land. There are one or two small areas of communal land and one instance of government-owned land
(40 ha). In one area hereditary tenants'' (lathbands) have rights to a portion of the land. Hereditary
tenancy is quite common in sailaba or khushkaba areas, and hereditary tenants will most probably control
a considerable proportion of the land in the proposed pilot flood irrigation schemes.
2.18       Farmers in perennial scheme areas are mainly owner-operators, although at some sites up
to a third of them are tenants. Tenants can be a mixture of hereditary tenants or tenants-at-will (seasonal
occupancy rights only). Potential flood irrigation sites may have a larger proportion of tenants. Tenancy
crop sharing arrangements vary considerably between potential scheme areas. Hereditary tenants receive
from 75 to 95% of production, while tenants-at-will usually receive 50%. This may fall as low as 33%,
depending on the inputs which the tenant supplies. Because of the high returns involved, orchard
tenancies usually give less than 50% to the tenant, and this share is often influenced by the area wage rate
for farm labor. While bonded tenancy-' exists in some sailaba and khushkaba areas, the project will try
to avoid areas with bonded-tenants.
2.19       Water Rights. At all screened perennial irrigation sites communities have distributed water
rights, and generally individuals own them. At one site the community holds all water rights, and gives
each male child born a water share which he can use during his lifetime. At death the share reverts to
the community. At one other site the community has distributed water rights to clans. The clans,
however, are expected to eventually distribute these rights to individual families. Water rights are usually
always held separately from land rights, and the ownership of water rights determines whether a farmer
can irrigate land. At all screened sites, however, the present distribution of water rights is basically
egalitarian with the top twenty-five percent of water right holders not owning more than 59% of the total
water rights. While originally communities distributed water rights equally, inheritance laws (generally
all sons receive an equal right) and unequal family size has led to the present distribution. Although
individuals rarely completely sell their water rights, markets do exist for water rights. Generally,
individuals may sell the use of their rights for a season, or a community, when it needs cash for repairs,
Hereditary tenancy occurs when land-owners (usually large) have their
tenants develop land for sailaba or khushkaba cultivation, that is,
level and build field bunds. In return the tenants obtained partial
ownership of the land (around 25%) and a permanent occupancy right
while he cares for the land. However even when the occupancy right
is terminated, the tenant retains ownership of his land share.
2/         Tenant is bonded to his landlord until he repays his debts, and crop
sharing arrangements are usually heavily weighted towards the
landlord.



- 16 -
may auction the use of rights which they still control, for example, higher than average water flows or
rights allocated specifically for operation and maintenance.
2.20       Because of the uncertainties of flood flows, sailaba farmers generally do not have specific
quantifiable water rights but rather a right to divert flood waters to their lands. At each sailaba diversion
site, communities have evolved a set of rules as to which groups can divert water, siting and size of
diversion structures, when a second irrigation is allowed and so on. While these regulations may or may
not be entered into the Record of Rights, customary law recognizes them, and law courts have upheld
them. Whether farmers actually divert water during a particular flood depends on the flood's size and
duration. Often large floods will breech or wash away group's diversion structures, or a flood is not
large enough to water all group's areas.
2.21       Water User Groups. All communities with irrigation supplies have informal or formal
water user groups or associations (WUAs). Formal WUAs exist only in government-financed irrigation
schemes, particularly in the On-Farm Water Management (OFWM) program, and BMIAD. Informal
WUAs may be strong and permanent or weak and temporary, depending largely on the equality or
otherwise of water and land distribution and the degree of cooperation between groups or clans. Even
where informal WUAs are strong, they tend to have two weaknesses. Firstly, the WUA leadership is
usually the same as the overall village or community leadership, and village problems with no relevance
for irrigation can affect the performance of the group. Secondly, local irrigator groups tend not to plan
activities, and thus their efficiency of operation may suffer. GOBal and the Bank Group initially
introduced formal WUAs under OFWM program and also in BMIAD, superimposing the formal WUA
on the traditional group. Generally, formal WUAs have failed, mainly because traditional leaders,
without training assistance, find it extremely difficult to adapt to the narrowly defined specialized
functions of a formal WUA, and the incentives to cooperate are inappropriate and inadequate. This is
particularly so under OFWM program where the one overriding incentive to join a formal WUA was
GOBal's construction subsidy. Once OFWM construction was completed, farmers no longer had an
incentive to manage the system effectively. Under BMIAD, GOBal has planned WUAs better,
establishing them around a single hydraulic unit, ensuring equity in water and land ownership and
introducing some training for the management and members. However, more needs to be done to ensure
formal WUAs sustainability. This includes considerable training to ensure management and members can
undertake their responsibilities, greater involvement by members in scheme, planning, design and
construction, including investment in scheme capital costs, and a formal statement of both WUA members
and government agency rights and obligations.
Land Use and Cropping Systems
2.22       Land Use. At all potential scheme sites farmers are already cropping land to the extent
possible with existing water supplies. Farmers are using practically all other land for livestock grazing
activities. With project development farmers would either intensify crop production from existing
cropped areas, convert suitable grazing land to cropping land, or in the higher, narrower valleys develop
suitable cropping land beside or near the main stream flow. This consists of bunding areas and collecting
silt from flood flows, or actually transporting top soil to the site.
2.23       Cropping Systems. Altitude mainly determines farmers' cropping systems, and at the
various potential perennial scheme sites you can recognize three general cropping systems. These include
cropping systems at high altitude sites (above 2,000 m), at medium altitude sites (1,300 m to 2,000 m),



- 17 -
and at low altitude sites (below 1,300 m). A fourth cropping system is found at flood diversion or sailaba
sites.
2.24        At high altitude sites farmers mainly grow apples plus some cherries, plums and pears. The
best quality apples come from these sites. They also grow small areas of hardy, short-season summer
vegetables, wheat, maize and fodder crops. Farmers often intercrop vegetables or fodder crops with
newly planted orchards until the trees shade-out further intercropping. Apples give the highest gross
margins, however, a few farmers have planted cherries which also can give high returns. Unlike apples,
cherry production is not yet well established. At medium altitude sites farmers still plant mainly
deciduous fruits, particularly apples. However, there is a wider spread of other fruits, and in favorable
localities farmers grow considerable amounts of apricots, grapes, almonds and pomegranates. Farmers
also grow summer vegetables, potato, wheat, barley and fodder crops such as alfalfa. At both high and
medium altitude sites farmers generally use their most reliable water supplies to grow orchards, and their
less reliable supplies to grow annual crops. This is particularly so with wheat, which is grown during
winter/spring when water requirements are usually smallest. In the past farmers developed mixed
orchards, particularly apples and apricots. New orchard plantings, however, are now practically always
a single fruit type, and mainly apples.
2.25        Farmers generally do not grow apples and other deciduous fruits below 1,300 m. Thus,
at low altitude sites in winter, farmers mainly crop wheat and small areas of fodder and vegetables, and
in summer, rice, sorghum, pulses, fodder, vegetables (particularly onions) and melons. At all altitudes
farmers usually have some livestock, and farmers' cropping patterns take account of their livestock needs.
This intertwining of cropping and livestock activities tends to be more pronounced at low altitude sites.
At sailaba sites in winter farmers grow wheat, and in summer mainly fodder sorghum, often interplanted
with pulses such as mung bean. They may also grow small areas of melons. Farmers highly integrate
their cropping and livestock activities, and livestock are an integral part of the farming system. The
returns from sailaba cropping are unreliable.
F. Existing Infrastructure and Services
Infrastructure
2.26        Balochistan has a poor infrastructural base. While Quetta is connected by road to other
major cities in Punjab, Sindh and North-West Frontier Province, the provincial road network is
inadequate, poorly maintained, and frequently damaged by floods. However, trucks can reach all
potential scheme sites so far screened, and adequate road access is a criteria for selecting future possible
sites. Quetta is also connected to Lahore and Karachi by rail, however, rail transport moves little or none
of the province's agricultural production. Road transport moves practically all agricultural commnodities
produced and most agricultural inputs used. While Balochistan has a vibrant private sector road transport
industry, delays in moving goods are common during rainy periods. Quetta is connected by air to other
provincial capitals, but intra-provincial air connections are poor.
2.27        Telecommunication services are mainly confined to the major towns, as are electricity
services. While the more accessible rural areas do also have electricity, the coverage is sparse and the
supply inadequate and unreliable. Domestic water supplies exist only in the larger towns, but are
generally inadequate. Balochistan has large natural gas supplies, but only a few towns other than Quetta
are connected to it.



- 18 -
Services
2.28        Balochistan has inadequate and poor health and education services. While Quetta and a few
large towns have health facilities, rural areas have few, and those that do exist are usually not staffed.
Education services are little better, and despite Balochistan recently building elementary schools in most
villages, many are not fully staffed. The province's literacy rate is 27% for men and 5 % for women
(national average, men 50% and women 17%).
2.29        Agricultural Services. Despite DOA employing a large staff, and possessing considerable
infrastructure in terms of offices, vehicles, equipment, research facilities and seed farms, Balochistan's
agricultural research and extension services are weak. This is mainly because of inadequate staff training,
unsuitable organization, poor management and insufficient annual operating funds. DOA's main research
institutes include an Agricultural Crop Research Institute and a Deciduous Fruit Development Center
(DFDC) at Sariab (Quetta outskirts), an Agricultural Research/Training Center in Nasirabad district, and
Adaptive Research Farms in Loralai, Nasirabad and Las Bela districts. DOA has agricultural extension
staff stationed in practically all districts who, in the past, mainly sold fertilizers and pesticides to farmers
plus sprayed crops and orchards for them. Under BAEAR, DOA introduced "training and visit" style
extension services to Loralai, Kachhi, Nasirabad and Las Bela districts. It reorganized and strengthened
extension staff in these districts, moving them out of input supply and services to concentrating on
supplying farmers advice and information on solving their priority production problems and the use of
relevant technology. In these districts BAEAR has successfully changed DOA's emphasis from input
supply to informational and farmer training services. So far, however, DOA's impact on encouraging
farmers to adopt improved, more remunitative technology appears small, and further changes appear
necessary to improve the quality of DOA's extension services. Among other things, this includes greater
farmer community involvement in planning the service, further reorganizing and strengthening of DOA
and in some instances changing or upgrading the level of extension staff directly meeting farmers in the
field. DOA implemented BAEAR in only two of this project's expected project area districts (Loralai
and Kachhi).
2.30        DOA, with UNDP and FAO support, has operated DFDC for the last some 15 years.
DFDC has done considerable adaptive research on finding improved deciduous fruit varieties and
cropping practices for Balochistan farmers. In particular it has identified semi-dwarf apple varieties
which grow well under Balochistan conditions (start producing around four years after planting versus
eight years for local varieties, and higher yields/ha than local varieties), and shown the benefits from
annual pruning of fruit trees. To date farmers have been slow to adopt this technology. This may be
due, however, to insufficient nursery production of the new varieties and inadequate demonstration and
promulgation of the economic benefits of pruning. The Agricultural Research II Project, which DOA
is presently implementing, is assisting DOA upgrade its research capacities and will provide further
assistance for horticultural research programs, particular in vegetable production.
2.31        Formal credit services do not exist, or are ineffective in most of the project area. To the
extent possible, farmers satisfy their credit requirements from informal sources. Fertilizer and pesticides
are generally available in the main towns. Farmers from remote villages, however, incur considerable
difficulty in purchasing them.



- 19 -
III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives
3.1         The project's overall objectives are to increase the scope and productivity of Balochistan's
minor irrigation subsector and increase farmers' incomes. Its specific objectives are to:
(a)   redefine government's role in minor irrigation development and provide assistance
to facilitate change;
(b)   develop sustainable FOs which, amongst other things, can efficiently operate and
maintain minor irrigation systems;
(c)   develop self-sustaining irrigation systems, and increase FO participation in their
planning and construction;
(d)   increase beneficiaries' incomes and alleviate poverty; and
(e)   increase the recovery of public expenditures on irrigation infrastructure from
beneficiaries.
B. Summary Description
3.2         The project initiates implementation of the government's new irrigation and drainage strategy
(para 1.22-23) for developing efficient, sustainable minor irrigation.  In doing this, it undertakes
interrelated actions to greatly increase community involvement in project development, financing and
operation so as to increase beneficiaries' ownership and commitment to project works, and lead to the
efficient and sustainable use of resources. These actions are in three broad areas, that is, developing and
strengthening communities so they can assist plan and construct minor irrigation systems and assume full
responsibility for operating them; constructing appropriate irrigation infrastructure which beneficiaries
would be prepared to properly operate and maintain; and changing and strengthening BID's capabilities
so it can assist communities to eventually take a leading role in developing their minor irrigation
resources. The individual project components are:
Community Development and Support (US$5.7 million)Y'
(a)   forming and developing FOs by providing technical assistance, data collection,
guidance, legislation and training;
(b)   providing engineering, agricultural and other services to project beneficiaries;
Including contingencies.



- 20 -
Infrastructure (US$20.0 million)
(c)   designing, constructing and/or rehabilitating some 30 minor irrigation schemes, a
pilot program of four flood irrigation schemes and, where possible, associated
potable water supplies;
Project Management (US$8.2 million)
(d)   supporting a project management unit (PMU) within BID to implement the project
by providing staff, technical assistance, training, equipment, vehicles, office space
and operating funds. The PMU would include a community support group (CSG);
(e)   monitoring and evaluating project implementation through providing staff, technical
assistance, vehicles, equipment, office space and operating funds;
Agency Strengthening (US$4.6 million)
(f)   strengthening BID's capabilities to interact with local communities and assist develop
community planned and implemented minor irrigation by providing technical
assistance and training, including implementing some eight mini irrigation schemes
as part of in-service training; and
(g)   developing a pilot program to investigate how BID might generally transfer O&M
responsibilities to FOs of all minor irrigation schemes outside this project or
BMIAD.
C. Project Details
Community Development and Support
3.3        Community and Scheme Selection. Under the project BID's PMU would screen all sites
proposed for entering the project according to social, technical and economic criteria. Major selection
criteria include: (a) no major unsolved disputes in the area; (b) top quartile of water right holders and
land owners should not control more than 70% of total water rights or own more than 70% of total
available land respectively; (c) schemes must have potential for increasing agricultural production;
(d) minor perennial schemes must have an assured minimum water supply of 1 ft3/sec (cusec), while mini
schemes' water supply would be less than 1 cusec; (e) scheme development must have a 10% or higher
estimated ERR; (f) beneficiaries must establish a formal FO which would open local FO bank accounts,
be involved in scheme design and construction, and responsible for scheme operation and maintenance;
and (g) beneficiaries must contribute from 15 to 30% of total scheme capital costs in real terms and 100%
of O&M costs (see paras 3.17-3.19).
3.4        PMU's screening procedures include a short reconnaissance visit to assess a scheme's
potential and then, if the scheme has promise, more detailed studies to decide whether to implement.
These studies cover social, technical and agricultural issues, and while undertaking them PMU staff
explain and discuss the project concepts with local leaders and community. If the scheme meets the
project criteria, and the community agrees to the development, then PMU staff request beneficiaries to



- 21 -
immediately establish a formal FO. As at all sites informal organizations already exist to operate the
irrigation system, the formal FO takes the place or builds on the informal group. If necessary to take
account of different tribal groupings or more than one water source, each with distinct water rights,
beneficiaries should form an apexed FO, or more than one FO. The project would assist irrigators to
adapt or move from a traditional water user group to a formal, modern FO.
3.5         FO Strengthening. The project would develop and strengthen formal FOs so they can
undertake the responsibilities required under the project, and become self-sustaining bodies. FO's
responsibilities include: (a) interacting and agreeing with PMU staff on the overall planning and design
of the scheme; (b) deciding and agreeing with PMU staff on the nature and scope of command area
development (CAD) works; (c) constructing CAD works; (d) operating and maintaining the irrigation
systems; (e) organizing and collecting beneficiaries' contributions to capital and O&M costs (including
scheme capital repayment funds), whether in cash or labor, and depositing the cash collected in the FO's
two bank accounts, that is, its Capital Development (CD) Account and O&M Account (see para 3.18);
and (f) in association with the relevant Deputy Commissioner and BID Executive Engineer, deciding and
agreeing on the use of scheme capital repayment funds. After the project the FO would be responsible
for future development of the irrigation systems, and could organize and procure services its members
required, for example, agricultural extension advice, agricultural chemicals, credit, group commodity
marketing and so on.
3.6         PMU's CSP staff would assist beneficiaries establish their FO, elect/appoint the necessary
office bearers (chairperson, secretary, treasurer), and open the necessary bank accounts. CSG, most
probably using an NGO such as BRSP, would train FO office bearers in group organization,
management, record-keeping and monitoring, and FO members as village development agents (VDA) (see
para 4.8). CSG staff themselves would provide FO members a wide range of services and training
including amongst others: (a) training in: (i) project financing, credit discipline and cost recovery; (ii)
construction techniques including planning, cost estimation, budgeting, materials procurement and quality
control; (iii) operating and maintaining the irrigation systems including providing an O&M manual; and
(iv) appropriate agricultural practices; (b) designs for CAD works and supervision of FO construction;
and (c) agricultural cropping advice including demonstrations, field days, and on-farm research. To give
FO members on-the-job training in construction work and contract administration, PMU would contract
with FOs to undertake simple portions of the main works, which would not impinge on completion of
main contract works, such as gabion construction and canal lining.
3.7        Women's Groups. In schemes which include a potable water supply (PWS), the project
would support and develop the formation of a women's group (WG) or groups to represent female PWS
beneficiaries. WGs would be responsible for: (a) giving input to PMU on PWS design; (b) assisting in
collecting fees for PWS operation and maintenance; (c) assisting in arranging for basic maintenance
activities, if necessary in conjunction with FO for any large maintenance activities; and (d) acting as a
conduit for PWS health and hygiene training and agricultural extension messages for agricultural activities
in which the local women engage. CSG staff would assist community women establish WGs and elect
their office bearers. If possible WGs and FOs should be closely aligned, with WG leader possibly being
