the wife of FO chairperson or other official. As for FOs, CSG would most probably use BRSP to train
WG officials in group organization, management, record-keeping and monitoring. CSG would train WG
members in basic PWS O&M and PWS health and hygiene, and also give village men a shortened version
of the health training. CSG would give WG members agricultural training and advice concerning those
agricultural activities for which the community women are responsible. In schemes without PWS, CSG



- 22 -
would investigate the possibility of establishing WGs as a means of increasing communication between
men and women, and providing, where appropriate, agricultural extension advice for women.
3.8        1981 Ordinance. To strengthen FOs functional sustainability, the project would provide
local technical assistance to revise the 1981 Balochistan Water Users Association Ordinance (1981
Ordinance) and produce a new draft ordinance more appropriate as the legal structure for FOs required
under this project. Though potentially valuable, the 1981 Ordinance is too restrictive in its application
and too rigid concerning operating rules (for example, periodicity of meetings, voting procedures, tenure
of officials and so on). A new ordinance should: (a) change FO registration point from the Director, On-
Farm Water Management, Department of Agriculture, to the Registrar of Forms, Department of Industry;
(b) allow FOs to stipulate their operating procedures through by-laws; (c) specifically cover small-scale
irrigation schemes; (d) include an option for FO to own irrigation infrastructure; and (e) allow FO to
undertake development work on behalf of the government. GOBal and IDA have agreed to a timetable
for gazetting the proposed new Balochistan Farmer Organization Ordinance. While during early project
implementation beneficiaries may continue to register their FOs under the 1981 Ordinance, immediately
the new ordinance is gazetted all project FOs would come under its jurisdiction. Other project actions
to strength FO functional sustainability include the FO and Project Director signing a formal scheme
agreement (see para 4.5); after completion of schemes' main works, the project would formally handover
responsibility for O&M to FO (para 3.10); and after FO repays its share of capital costs, GOBal would
transfer ownership of scheme works to FO (para 4.6).
Irrigation Infrastructure
3.9        Minor Irrigation. The project would construct and or rehabilitate about 30 minor irrigation
schemes. To the extent possible project staff would select schemes which are clustered in reasonably
close proximity to each other (groups of two to five) and near the towns of Zhob, Loralai, Sibi and
Khuzdar. Project works would consist of main works, that is, headwork, and all or major portions of
main and secondary canals (including structures and offtakes) which PMU would design and construct,
and CAD works, that is, any remaining portions of the main or secondary canals and works to deliver
water to the farm which PMU/CSG would design but FO construct. Head works or water diversion
structures could consist of weirs, infiltration galleries, free intakes or low-lift pumps. The project would
not rehabilitate karezes or install tubewells, however, PMU would accept a properly functioning and
sustainable kareze (kareze owners can stop tubewell or well sinking in kareze water source area) as a
scheme water supply. PMU would determine the cut-off point between main and CAD works by
economically analyzing the extent to which it should line the canal system, using contractors and concrete
or masonry materials. The point where marginal cost of this lining equals marginal revenue would be
the start of CAD works. This point may be on the main or secondary canals. PMU would, however,
apply this calculation flexibility so that CAD works do not contain construction beyond FO capabilities.
CAD works could consist of constructing or improving canals, division structures and farm off-takes.
It could also include some canal lining using alternative materials to concrete, and other measures to
improve water management efficiency. From detailed design work already undertaken, CAD works are
a little more than 2% of total scheme construction costs. In designing scheme structures, PMU would
emphasize functionality, simplicity of design and least cost alternative, including where appropriate,
pumps as a water intake. PMU would contract local contractors and farmers (see para 3.6) to construct
the main works while FOs would hire or contract local farmers or artisans to construct CAD works.



- 23 -
3.10       While constructing the main works PMU/CSG would train FO members in O&M
techniques. Immediately following main works completion, BID would formally transfer main works
O&M responsibilities to FOs, and provide FOs a maintenance manual. A two year "aftercare" period
would then commence during which CSG would further train FO members in O&M and water
management issues, design CAD works and assist FOs construct them. However, CSG would allow the
increased discharges to flow for about a year before undertaking this design to allow farmers to observe
and experience the new discharge and decide how to manage it. Based on this experience, farmers could
then decide whether to adjust or completely change their water rotation schedule and agree on the CAD
works required for proper water management. To allow PMU to undertake the minor irrigation
construction, the project would provide civil works, vehicles, equipment, staff, technical assistance,
training and operating costs.
3.11       Potable Water Supply. Where possible and needed, the project would provide a PWS
along with the minor irrigation works. Criteria for including PWS are: (a) perennial irrigation scheme
must have a reliable, clean water source such as a kareze, spring or infiltration gallery so that water
requires no treatment to be potable; (b) villages must not have existing improved water supplies;
(c) villages must be suppliable by gravity; (d) FO or water-right holders must agree to all community
members having access to PWS even during months of peak agricultural water requirements; and
(e) community members must want PWS and be prepared to: (i) form WGs who would be responsible,
along with a PWS Operation Committee, for assisting to design system layout, undertaking O&M and
collecting community O&M contributions; (ii) receive health training in PWS; and (iii) contribute 100%
of O&M costs. Based on the nine screening reports so far available, the project could construct PWS
at about two-thirds of the minor irrigation scheme sites.
3.12       PMU engineers would design PWSs according to GOBal's current guidelines, that is, a 45
litres/head/day design demand with 20% wastage and 3 % population growth rates, and a 20 year design
life. Adding a peak water requirement to the design equal to three times the average requirements means
that in year 20 PWS demand as a percentage of the total flow available at the screened sites would vary
from about 2.5% to 9.0%. PWS systems would extract water from inside the source (kareze, spring or
infiltration gallery) to reduce the risk of contamination, and convey it to villages by gravity through
plastic or asbestos cement pipes, usually laid adjacent to the main irrigation channel. The project would
provide community stand pipes at suitable points along the pipeline, with a facility to drain waste-water
back into the irrigation channel. Any individual villager wanting a PWS house connection would have
to pay 100% of the connection costs from the pipeline. The project would construct a dead end
connection to the front of the individual's house and equip it with a gate valve and gate valve chamber.
The villager would be responsible for any reticulation of the water into his compound or house. In
principle the project would connect the public stand pipes directly to the delivery pipeline. However, in
certain instances where demand is likely to have pronounced peaks, the project may have to construct
buffer reservoirs, which would be kept small to ensure a rapid turnover of water to reduce the risk of
the water quality deteriorating. CSG staff would encourage community members to use PWS for drinking
only, and not for washing clothes or kitchen gardening. PMU would design clothes washing and animal
drinking facilities into the irrigation channel system. To enable PMU implement this component, the
project would provide civil works, vehicles, equipment, staff, technical assistance, training and operating
costs.
3.13       Pilot Flood Irrigation Schemes. The project would construct four pilot flood irrigation
schemes to test the social, technical and economic feasibility of constructing permanent, simple, low cost



- 24 -
flash flood diversion structures which would reduce system maintenance costs and improve diversion
efficiency. PMU would design the structures to: (a) enable flood irrigators to utilize small and medium
floods while allowing high floods to pass virtually unchecked; (b) reduce and simplify annual
maintenance; (c) improve access to river flows during recession of high flows which partially breach
diversion structure; and (d) regulate flows into the main conveyance channel to reduce flood damage in
the command area, incorporate sediment control measures and improve efficiency of the existing
distribution network.
3.14       The main structure is a short weir with an earth bund across the remainder of the river
channel. This would control low river flows. The weir is designed so that a medium flood with a two
or three year probability could safely pass over it. Larger floods, however, would breach the bund,
allowing flood waters to bypass the weir and leave it intact. The diversion structure would also
incorporate a head regulator to limit flows into the conveyance channel during high floods, and a sand
sluice to reduce sediment ingress and facilitate accumulated silt removal. To minimize construction costs,
PMU would contract with FOs to build the earthen structures.
3.15       In selecting the three scheme sites, PMU would follow the same social and equity criteria
as para 3.3 mentions, with the additional provision that preferably none, but at the most no more than
5% of the command area farmers should be bonded tenants. The frequency and magnitude of floods
would have to be sufficient to justify developing the site, and the overall development must have an
estimated 10% ERR or higher, or a strong potential for a 10% ERR. Soils in the command area should
have a minimum six foot depth and be fine textured with a moisture holding capacity of at least 18 % by
volume. If more than one irrigation can be applied during a season, these moisture holding criteria could
be relaxed. As this is a pilot program, PMU would not request farmers to repay any capital costs,
however, at completion of construction PMU would hand the scheme over to FO for O&M. Should the
structures prove viable, FOs would be expected to continue operating and maintaining them. To enable
PMU to implement this component, the project would provide civil works, vehicles, equipment, staff,
technical assistance, training and operating costs.
3.16       Status of Engineering. During project preparation studies, BID registered 309 perennial
minor irrigation schemes for possible funding under the project. Following initial reconnaissance of 180
schemes, BID staff classified 11 for screening studies, 61 for further consideration and 108 as unsuitable.
BID has now completed nine screening studies, and for six of these schemes detailed design including
detailed cost estimates and preparation of tender documents. BID staff, with technical assistance funded
by GON, will continue to screen schemes and prepare detailed designs up to project commencement,
expected around August 1995. BID has also completed detailed feasibility reports for four flood
irrigation schemes. Because of the intricacies of flood flows and traditional rules governing water
allocations between groups, a screening report presents insufficient information to decide whether to
implement a flood scheme. Thus BID has undertaken larger feasibility reports for this purpose.
3.17       Community Contribution. The project would provide for beneficiaries to contribute to the
capital costs of minor and mini irrigation schemes, and to the O&M costs of all development works.
While beneficiaries would contribute 100% of all O&M costs, they would pay either 15 or 30% of
scheme capital costs in real terms depending upon the ability of beneficiaries to pay. The project would
use the percentage of scheme service area planted to producing orchard as an indicator of existing average
financial returns per cropped ha. Scheme service areas with 2% or less planted to producing orchards



- 25 -
would be considered "low income" areas, and beneficiaries would pay 15 % of capital costs. Beneficiaries
from all other service areas (greater than 2% producing orchard) would pay 30% of capital costs.
3.18       Table 3.1 shows beneficiary contributions towards the minor and mini irrigation scheme
costs, including timing, form and amount of payments. Beneficiary payments would be linked to specific
milestones in the planning and implementation process so that project management could halt development
should beneficiaries fail to fulfill their obligations, for example, PMU will not commence scheme detailed
design until beneficiaries have deposited the required funds into their CD account. During design and
construction, beneficiaries from low income schemes would pay 7% of capital costs, in either cash or
labor contributions, but with a minimum of 1 % cash. Following completion of the main works, they
would repay another 8 % in cash over about five years. They would start these payments immediately
after the second cropping season (that is, one year later) following satisfactory completion of the main
works. During this grace period farmers will neither repay capital costs nor pay interest on their
unrepaid balances. During design and construction beneficiaries from other schemes would pay 15 % of
capital costs, also in either cash or labor contributions, but with a minimum of 9% cash. Following
completion of their main works, they would repay another 15% in cash over about six years, including
a one year grace period. In both cases, farmer repayments would include a 12% interest rate charge on
their unrepaid capital cost balances.
3.19       When beneficiaries contribute their labor to project costs, they will work under a formal
contract with PMU. Although their labor will be free, PMU will pay contracted beneficiaries an amount
equivalent to the contractor's overhead so they have funds for daily subsistence. FOs would collect all
cash capital cost payments and repayments and deposit them into a development fund bank account. The
up-front cash payments would be used to mainly finance the main works, but could also be used to
finance CAD works. Cash repayments would be used to finance CAD works and high priority
community development works including further irrigation works. However, the relevant Deputy
Commissioner or his representitive, the relevant BID Executive Engineer, and FO representatives would
have to agree to these community development works, and to withdrawals from the development funds
to finance them. As beneficiaries would contribute 100% of all irrigation O&M costs, GOBal would
exempt them from paying abiana (existing water charges) on their lands within scheme service areas.
Strengthening Project Management
3.20       PMU Support. To ensure strong implementing management with multidisciplinary skills,
the project would support and expand a PMU within BID to implement the project. The PMU's main
objective would be to timely implement the project while ensuring strong community involvement in
project planning, implementation and operation. BID originally established the PMU to implement
BMIAD, and will retain and reorganize it for this project. To provide greater expertise in community
and FO development and training, BID will add to PMU a separate community support group (CSG)
which would include four field offices. PMU would have two other main groups, an administration
group directly under the Project Director and comprising two sections, Accounts and Administration
(AAS) and Management Information Systems (MIS); and an engineering group headed by an Assistant
Project Director (BPS'' Grade 19) and comprising another two sections, Planning and Design (PDS) and
Construction, Supervision and Coordination (CSCS)(see Chart 1).
1/         Base Pay Scale Grade 19.

when GON and KfW funded BMIAD activities finish. Chart 2 and Annex 2, Table 1 show for this
project all proposed PMU staff positions, and sources of staff. Internationally and locally recruited
consultants would mainly staff PDS, while CSG staff would consist of three main groups, that is:
sociologists (men and women) who would act as social or farner organizers and would assist establish,
develop and train FOs and WGs; irrigation engineers, trained in water management and development of
small farmer irrigation systems who would assist, design, supervise and train farmers when constructing
CAD works, undertaking contract works on main works, and operating the irrigation systems during the
aftercare period; and agricultural experts and extensionists who would advise and assist farmers with
cropping patterns, crop production and crop water requirements. CSG would locate most of its staff in
field offices to work directly with the communities. Paras 4.5-4.9 describe in more detail community
and CSG interactions. Besides using seconded staff from BID and DOA, PMU would directly hire most
of its staff from the local market for the duration of the project. To ensure these staff are quickly in
place, and do not have a basis for requesting permanent employment with BID, the internationally
recruited consultant firm would recruit, manage and administer most of the directly hired staff for PMU.
IDA obtained an assurance from GOBal that by December 31, 1995 PMU would recruit and place all
its higher level (BPS-16 and above) seconded staff, that is, from BID a Project Director (BPS-20),
Assistant ProjectDirector (BPS-I9), AssistantDirector Finance (BPS-18), fourArea Engineers (BPS-18),
and four Junior Engineers (BPS-1 7), and from DOA six Agricultural Officers (BPS-i 7). The project
would provide CSG four field offices, located at Zhob, Loralai, Sibi and Pishin. These offices would
not be permanent, and CSG would rent buildings for them. Chart 2 and Annex 2, Table 1 show the staff
at each field office. Besides CSG staff, CSCS would station its construction supervision engineers at the
offices. To assist PMU undertake its implementation responsibilities, the project would provide rented
office buildings, furniture, office and field equipment, computers (plus software and air conditioners),
vehicles (see Annex 2, Table 2), additional professional and support staff, internationally and locally
recruited technical assistance and incremental operating funds. While project management costs are high,
not all expenditures allocated to this component are administration and management costs. A considerable
proportion are for the design of the irrigation infrastructure. However, these management expenditures
reflect the costs of working with small, scattered communities, of attempting to develop sustainable FOs,
and of involving FOs in project planning and operation.
Institutional Strengthening
3.23       The project would assist BID to commence re-orienting its programs so that it can interact
more with local communities and bring them fully into the planning, implementation and operation of
minor irrigation development. BID's objective would be to eventually change its role from a constructor
of minor irrigation to a supplier of technical services to FOs wishing to construct and rehabilitate minor
irrigation. In doing this the project would provide BID resources to: (a) develop a training strategy to
transform itself from a traditional engineering, expert department within the civil service to a customer-
oriented provider of technical services, perhaps competing in future with commercial consultant firms;
(b) implement a series of training courses for all levels of staff; (c) send appropriate staff on international
study tours to countries which have encouraged client orientation in irrigation development; (d) implement
a hands-on training program for BID staff by undertaking the development of eight mini irrigation
schemes; and (e) develop a pilot program to transfer O&M responsibilities from BID to the beneficiaries
of all other minor irrigation schemes outside this project and BMIAD. Additionally, BID's relevant circle
field staff would be responsible for supervising the construction of one minor irrigation scheme. As
project schemes are expected to be in four circles, this would involve a total of four schemes. When



- 28 -
completed, BID and IDA would review and assess the work, and propose further measures to strengthen
BID's capabilities to work with communities.
3.24        Training Strategy.  BID senior management, assisted by an internationally recruited
training expert and with inputs from all staff levels, would develop BID's training strategy. To help
develop the strategy, the training expert would undertake an extensive training needs analysis of BID's
staff. It is expected that the strategy would include: (a) core training to provide staff skills not normally
obtained in their academic studies such as construction supervision and contract administration; (b)
professional/primary training to improve staff skills in specific engineering areas such as successful
management of groundwater or design of infiltration galleries; (c) strategic management training to
facilitate BID's change process to a provider of technical services to FOs. Senior BID management
would be encouraged to develop management plans for the future, including a business plan with
identified objectives and strategies; (d) specific "customer focus" training to improve staff understanding
of customer relationships, agricultural practices, and farmer needs, and the necessity of increasing
community consultation to increase farmer input into project planning, design and implementation;and
(e) foreign study tours to review successful community irrigation elsewhere.
3.25        Training courses will be provided in varying formats ranging from short on-site individual
instruction and short courses in BID's divisions to longer three to five day courses with wider staff
representation. - MIS staff will monitor training and training impact. To support the program, the project
would provide funds for 15 sm of internationally recruited training experts, 12 workshops, 900 staff days
of training courses, and for some 15-18 staff to undertake two week foreign study tours to Mexico, the
Philippines and the Dominican Republic.
3.26        Mini Irrigation Schemes. Following the same criteria and operating procedures used for
minor irrigation schemes, BID irrigation circle (district) staff would design, contract and supervise
construction of eight mini irrigation schemes. This would give them hands-on experience implementing
minor irrigation following the project's operating procedures. CSG staff would undertake the necessary
social, technical and agricultural development of the beneficiaries, while PMU engineering staff would
assist and supervise the field staff's design and construction work. If possible, oni at least two mini
schemes, FOs, with CSG assistance, would be responsible for constructing the main works, that is,
contracting out the works. The project would provide funds for equipment and civil works.
3.27        Transfer of O&M Responsibilities. PMU, alongwith BID staff, would further develop
BID's inventory of minor irrigation schemes. It would undertake a preliminary survey to categorize the
schemes by sociological factors, size of works, water flow, irrigated area and extent of rehabilitation
required. PMU would then statistically analyze the schemes and select a representative sample for
detailed inspection. PMU would develop and implement a pilot program of O&M transfer for some 9
to 12 schemes of various sizes, degrees of deterioration and/or other significant transfer factors. This
program would enable BID to further analyze the factors likely to effect the successful design and
implementation of a future larger project. To support the program, the project would provide funds for
the preliminary survey, and US$1.0 million equivalent to implement the pilot O&M transfer program.
The project consultants, particularly the team leader, would assist design the program. PMU may involve
the NGO, BRSP, in implementing the program.



- 29 -
Training and Technical Assistance
3.28       Training. Training is integral to the project. It would be directed at three main groups:
CSG social, agricultural and engineering staff to train the trainers; FO and WG members to establish
viable, sustainable organizations and improve irrigation and agricultural efficiency; and BID staff to
change BID work orientation. Except for some foreign study tours to observe sucessful comnmunity
irrigation systems, project training is structured around short-term in-service courses. These would
mainly consist of workshops and one to five day programs. Staff would do much of the farmner training
in the field, particularly in irrigation construction and O&M. BID would internationally recruit training
experts who would undertake training need assessments of CSG and BID staff, and then with the
assistance of other consultants hired under the project, develop and implement the required training. CSG
would use an NGO such as BRSP to train CSG social organizers, FO and WG office bearers and VDAs.
CSG's social, agricultural and engineering staff mainly would train community, FO and WG members.
While farmer training is difficult to aggregate, Annex 3, Table 1 shows the project's main foreign and
local training programs. IDA obtained assurances from GOBal that PMU would: (a) by January 31, 1996
furnish proposals for any NGO-implemented training under the project to IDA for its prior review and
approval; (b) by April 30, 1996 under conditions acceptable to IDA, sign a memorandum of
understanding with an appropriate NGO to supply training services; and (c) furnish proposals for other
training under the project to IDA for its prior review and approval.
3.29       Technical Assistance.  To assist PMU  in implementing the project, BID  would
internationally recruit some 173 staff months (snmIR) and locally recruit some 1,009 staff months (smLR)
of technical assistance as follows:
(a)  for CSG:
(i)    sociologists to assist CSG staff implement their program of selecting suitable
communities and establishing and developing sustainable FOs (24 smIR and
40 smLR);
(ii)    agricultural extensionists (agronomist) to plan and implement agricultural
extension, farmer training and on-farm research programs (12 smIR and 36
smLR);
(iii)    training planner to assist develop training programs for CSG's engineering,
agricultural and social development staff, including undertaking training needs
analyses (4 smIR);
(iv)    land and water management specialist to undertake adaptive research,
evaluation, development and implementation of alternative techniques for canal
lining and water management, plus assist develop water management training
programs (19 smIR); and
(v)     legal expert to review Balochistan 1981 Ordinance and draft a new Balochistan
Farmer Organization Ordinance (4 smLR);



- 30 -
(b)   for PMU's administration group a monitoring and evaluation expert to assist design
and implement appropriate monitoring and impact evaluation systems for all project
components (7 smIR);
(c)   for PMU's engineering group:
(i)     team  leader (irrigation and rural development engineer) to manage the
planning and design section and overall technical assistance input, including
assisting with a preliminary survey of remaining minor irrigation schemes for
which BID undertakes O&M, and developing a pilot program for O&M
transfer (60 smIR);
(ii)    irrigation design engineer/hydrologist (24 smiR) and agricultural economists
(8 smIR and 36 smLR) to assist prepare the screening and other studies,
undertake detailed design and advise and assist with project implementation;
(iii)    team to design minor, mini and flood irrigation schemes and PWS systems,
consisting of a project manager (32 smLR), design engineers (93 smLR),
specialist design engineers (6 smLR), hydrologist (11 smLR), soil scientist
(8 smLR), water supply engineer (11 smLR), survey engineers (124 smLR),
auto CAD (computer assisted design) technicians (93 smLR), and assistant
surveyors (124 smLR);
(iv)     chief supervising engineer (39 smLR) and site engineers (312 smnLR) to assist
supervise construction of works;
(v)     training experts to undertake a training needs assessment, and assist develop
and implement a training strategy and programs to change BID's work
orientation (15 smIR); and
(vi)    experts as required to assist supervise project implementation (20 smLR) and
prepare future projects (20 smnLR).
3.30        By July 31, 1995 GOBal through PMU would have internationally recruited a single
consultant firm or consortium of firms to supply all the services paragraph 3.29 lists except the
consultants to assist with project supervision and the preparation of future projects {para 3.29(c)(vi)}.
The firm or consortium should associate itself with a suitable local firm to supply the required locally
recruited consultants. If necessary to supply expertise in a particular area, the firm may subcontract a
component to an individual or firm or entity. Annex 3, Table 2 lists all technical assistance by PMU
section and expertise, and Table 3 lists technical assistance by classification, objectives, activities and
outputs. The IDA credit would not become effective until PMU has signed a contract with an
internationally recruitedfirm, or consortium offirms, with qualifications and under conditions acceptable
to IDA, to supply the services which para 3.29(a) (i-v), (b) and (c) (i-v) lists, and the consultants are on
location ready to commence work. IDA obtained an assurance from GOBal that PMU would locally
recruit consultants or consultant firms, with qualifications and under conditions acceptable to IDA to
supply the services which para 3.29(c) (vi) lists.



- 31 -
Project Phasing
3.31        BID would implement project works during the approximately five years July 1995 to June
30, 2000. Annex 4 shows key dates for activities crucial to project implementation during the first two
project years. These activities include undertaking a project launch workshop, recruiting incremental
staff, procuring vehicles and equipment, continuing screening studies and detailed design of irrigation
schemes, and procuring civil work contractors. Project staff would have consultants in the field by credit
effectiveness. As BID would finish constructing minor irrigation schemes during the project's fifth year,
these last schemes would still require a two-year aftercare period. This aftercare, however, would be
outside the project period which IDA would assist finance.
D. Cost Estimates and Financing
Cost Estimates
3.32        GOBal would fully implement the project over seven years. The IDA credit would assist
finance the project over the first five years. For this period the total estimated project cost is US$38.5
million, of which US$11.9 million is the foreign exchange costs. GOBal with GON assistance would
finance all project costs (US$1.5 million) during year six and seven. Project costs are expressed in
March 1995 prices, and include duties and taxes estimated at US$2.8 million. Base cost estimates were
derived from detailed cost analysis of recent GOBal agency operations (particularly under BMIAD
project), including staff training programs, irrigation and other construction contracts and vehicle and
equipment purchases. Contract quantities for irrigation works are based on detailed designs for six minor
irrigation schemes; on the average quantities required for these six schemes for the remaining 32
irrigation schemes; and on detailed feasibility studies for the three flood irrigation schemes. Contract
quantities for building construction come from standard government designs. Consultant service costs
are based on recent contracts for similar services in Balochistan and Pakistan. The cost estimates include
10% allowances for physical contingencies on civil works, machinery, equipment, training, and on
recurrent O&M costs associated with civil works, materials, equipment, vehicles and other administrative
overheads.   Additionally, the cost estimates include allowances for price escalations during
implementation based on IDA's estimated domestic and foreign inflation rates as shown in Table 3.2.

Financing
3.34        Table 3.4 shows the proposed project financing plan.  IDA, GON, GOBal and local
cDommunity farmers and households would jointly finance the project. IDA would provide a credit of
SDR 18.5 million (US$26.7 million), GON a grant of NLG 8.5 million (US$5.4 million), local
community farmers and households Rs 92.4 million (US$2.6 million), of which roughly Rs 47.6 million
would be in cash and Rs 44.8 million in labor, and GOBal the remaining Rs 135.1 million (US$3.8
million). IDA's credit would finance 69% of project costs, including 77% of foreign exchange costs and
66% of local costs. Excluding duties and taxes, IDA and GON together would finance 90% of project
costs. GOBal would provide its share of project finances to the implementing agency BID, including
funds for DOA's activities, out of annual budget appropriations. The federal Ministry of Finance will
allocate the project credit funds to GOBal as part of GOBal's annual development expenditure funds. The
credit will not become effective until: (a) the Executive Committee of the National Economic Council
(ECNEC) has approved the project's PC-I and sanctioned project expenditures; and (b) all conditions
precedent to the effectiveness of GON's Grant Agreement have been fulfilled, other than effectiveness of
IDA 's credit agreement.

documents for goods. The remaining equipment, furniture and materials would be needed at different
places, various times and in small quantities, and would not attract foreign bidders. PMU would procure,
through LCB procedures acceptable to IDA, electronic equipment, furniture and agricultural materials
which could be packaged into contracts of US$50,000 equivalent or more, up to an aggregate limit of
US$0.4 million. Equipment, furniture and materials costing less than US$50,000 equivalent, and up to
an aggregate limit of US$0.3 million, PMU could procure through local or international shopping
(minimum three price quotations).
3.38       Consultant Services, Studies and Training (6.0 million). PMU would procure consultant
services and studies (US$0.1 million) according to IDA guidelines for the use of consultants. PMU
would also use IDA guidelines when procuring consultants which GON would finance (US$5.4 million).
Documents used for inviting proposals, terms of reference for all consultancies and single-source contracts
will be subject to prior review for all contracts valued at US$100,000 or more awarded to firms and
US$50,000 or more awarded to individuals. Training arrangements (US$0.5 million) would be subject
to IDA approval.
3.39       Project Implementing Costs (US$5.3 million). Following normal government procedures,
PMU would procure project expenditures on operating and maintenance costs of vehicles, equipment and
tools (US$2.3 million) and incremental administrative overheads (US$0.8 million). PMU, and the
consultants appointed under the project would procure implementing personnel (direct hire staff) (US$2.2
million) for PMU at local market costs. FOs would 100% finance and procure project recurrent costs,
that is, civil works O&M expenditures (US$0.4 million).
3.40       IDA Review. IDA would prior review all documentation before tendering and award for
all ICB contracts, all contracts valued at US$200,000 equivalent or more for civil works, and all contracts
valued at US$50,000 equivalent or more for goods and training services. These reviews would cover
about 70 % of the value of civil works and 85 % of goods and training. Bank staff would selectively post
review some 10% of the awarded contracts which are below these threshold levels. Except for CAD
works and civil work's O&M, BID's PMU would procure all other civil works and goods under the
project.  PMU should handle this responsibility well as it had considerable similar procurement
experience under BMIAD, and will be staffed by consultants who will prepare tender documents and
assist in the overall procurement process. CSG staff would assist FOs implement their procurement
responsibilities. Annex 5 shows the project's procurement implementation schedule.
F. Disbursements
3.41       The credit would be disbursed over six years, with the credit closing June 30, 2001. There
are no standard disbursement profiles for irrigation or natural resource management projects in Pakistan
with which to compare the proposed disbursement profile (Annex 1, Table 5). However it is less than
the eight years standard disbursement profile for all Pakistan agriculural projects. This is appropriate as
the project is small, simple in design, and would be implemented in only one province.

IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Project Management
General
4.1        BID, through its PMU and in association with FOs and DOA, would be responsible for
implementing the project. GOBal would establish a project steering committee to determine overall
project policy, assist with coordination and regularly review imnplementation. The Additional Chief
Secretary, P&D, would chair the committee which would include, among others, the Secretaries of
Finance, Irrigation, Agriculture, Livestock, Health and Public Health Engineering, Member, Board of
Revenue and the Project Director. The steering committee would meet twice a year, or more often if
necessary, and would appove scheme selection, designs, workplans and budgets. PMU would act as the
committee's secretariat, and would prepare its agenda and monitor implementation of its decisions.
BID's Role
4.2        While BID's Secretary would be ultimately responsible for project operations, PMU's
Project Director would be the day-to-day project manager and PMU's three groups, together with FOs,
would plan, design and implement the project (paras 3.20-22).  To allow PMU undertake its
responsibilities, BID would enlarge, strengthen and reorganize it (para 3.20) including seconding senior
irrigation and other staff to it (see Annex 2). However, following completion of the final aftercare period
(project year seven), BID would disband PMU and bring the responsibilities for community irrigation
development back into BID's regular work programs. To prepare for this, PMU's PDS would assist
BID's management to formulate and implement a staff training strategy including management and
business plans (para 3.24); BID circle staff, with assistance from PMU, would implement eight mini
irrigation schemes (para 3.26); and PMU staff would assist BID staff plan and implement a pilot program
to transfer O&M responsibilities to FOs (para 3.27).
4.3        PMU would liaise with and be IDA's main contact concerning project implementation, and
would monitor overall project progress. It would prepare and submit to IDA half-yearly project reports
which would update and assess implementation of project components during the previous six months.
PMU and IDA would agree on the report format by end-November 1995, and PMU would forward the
reports to IDA within two months from the end of each six-month period, beginning with the semester
ending December 31, 1995. PMU would ensure that all credit disbursement applications are sent to IDA.
Within six months of credit completion, PMU would contribute to a project implementation completion
report, reporting on all aspects of the project. Additionally within this time span, PMU would prepare
a plan for the future operation of the project and submit it to IDA. LDA obtained assurances from GOBal
that PMU would: (a) within six months of credit completion prepare, under guidelines acceptable to IDA,
a plan for the future operation of the project; go) afford IDA the opportunity to exchange views with
GOBal on the plan; and (c) implement the plan with due diligence and efficiency, taking into account
IDA's comments on implementation.
DOA's Role
4.4        DOA will second six of its regular staff to PMU's CSG. These will be agricultural officer
level (AO)(BPS-17) extension staff, preferably with horticultural experience. CSG will station them in



- 38 -
its four field offices, and they will be an integral part of CSG's agricultural planning, operations and
extension programs. DOA field assistants (FAs) stationed in or near the irrigation scheme areas would
assist AOs implement the project's extension program. To enable FAs to be involved in project activities,
the project would provide DOA 50 motorcycles, and FAs a travel allowance when visiting project
farmers.
B. Implementation
Scheme Implementation
4.5        Besides scheme development applications already received during project preparation, PMU
will advertise the project again in the media immediately after project commencement. PMU's PDA and
CSG staff would screen and select suitable community schemes. Selected communities must agree to
project conditions, including establishing formal FOs, opening CD and O&M accounts at a local bank
and making an initial deposit into the CD account. CSG social organizers would assist communities form
and develop their FOs. Concurrently the Project Director and the FO, in the presence of the Deputy
Commissioner, would sign a scheme agreement which would detail the obligations and responsibilities
of both the project and FO. To ensure both parties enter into this agreement, IDA obtained assurances
from GOBal that before proceeding to implement any irrigation scheme, the Project Director and FO,
in the presence of the Deputy Commissioner or other appropriate official would sign an agreement which
is acceptable to IDA, and which would describe both parties' obligations and responsibilities under the
project. Annex 6 shows a draft scheme agreement.
4.6        PDS, in consultation with FO and inputs from CSG, would design scheme works.
Following FO approval of the works, and deposit of the required FO member funds in its CD account,
PMU would tender and award the main works. Following satisfactory completion of the main works,
FO would assume full O&M responsibility for the works. A two year aftercare period would then
commence during which FO, with CSG engineering staff assistance, would decide CAD works required
and construct them. Additionally, over the five to six years following completion of the main works, in
addition to earlier capital cost payments deposited into its CD account, FO would repay another 8 or 15%
in real terms of the scheme's capital cost (see paras 3.17-3.19). Following the successful completion of
these payments, BID would transfer to FO ownership of the constructed works. IDA obtained assurances
from GOBal that: (a) PMU, in consultation and agreement with FOs, shall identify, plan and prepare
project scheme proposals in a manner and in a form satisfactory to IDA; and (b) when an FO successfully
completes its agreed contributions towards scheme capital costs, BID would transfer to it the full
ownership rights over the improved headworks and distribution system.  Chart 3 shows the
implementation schedule for an individual minor irrigation scheme.
Social Development Program
4.7        CSG's social development staff would consist of an internationally recruited social scientist
(24 sm), a locally recruited social scientist (40 sm), a directly hired farmer organization specialist and
two directly hired women social organizers (one senior position, one junior position) in the field support
staff at Quetta; and two male farmer (social) organizers (one senior) and one female social organizer at
each field office (total of 12 social organizers in field offices). CSG would recruit local women for the
field offices, and they would live at home. During scheme screening, the social organizers would explain
the project concept, rights and obligations to community leaders and members. To undertake screening



- 39 -
studies, they would live in the community village area for about two weeks and collect data on
community population, occupations, wealth and income distribution, water and land rights distribution,
occurrence of disputes, existing cropping patterns and yields, livestock ownership, women's tasks, health
and so on. When project staff and the community decide to develop a proposed scheme, the social
organizers would assist the community establish and operate a formal FO (see para 3.6), and act as an
interface between the community and other project and government staff. The social organizers, in
particular, would assist FO develop its financial activities concerning scheme financing, cost recovery and
O&M. They would help FO organize its members to undertake its responsibilities under the project, for
example, receiving training from CSG staff, undertaking PMU contract work, and contracting out CAD
works. They would assist FO members with dispute mediation. Women social organizers would assist
community women establish and operate WGs, provide PWS O&M and health and hygiene training, and
organize appropriate agricultural training for women (see para 3.7). The social organizers, along with
other CSG staff, would be involved in project monitoring. The internationally recruited social scientist,
along with the senior women social organizer, would lead, plan and supervise the team's program. At
project commencement, an NGO such as BRSP would train the social organizers (both male and female)
in group formation, social assessment and other aspects of their work.
Water Management Program
4.8        CSG's water management team would consist of an internationally recruited land and water
management specialist (19 sm) and, directly hired, a senior water management engineer and two CAD
design engineers, in the field support staff at Quetta; and one water management and one farmer support
engineer in each field office. During the scheme aftercare period, the field office water management
engineer would advise and assist FO members identify the CAD works required to handle the increased
discharges from the improved main works. He/she would undertake trials and demonstrations of
alternative low-cost canal lining, and improved water management and application methods. The engineer
would also train FO members in water management, and O&M of the whole system. The farmer support
engineer would assist and supervise FO members in their CAD construction. The CAD design engineers
would design the agreed CAD works, while the senior water management engineer would assist, review
and supervise the CAD design work. The land and water management specialist would advise on and
undertake on-farm research of alternative low-cost canal lining and simple, improved water handling
techniques at the canal and field levels.
Agricultural Development Program
4.9        CSG's agricultural development staff would consist of an internationally recruited
agricultural extension specialist (12 sm), a locally recruited agricultural extension specialist (36 sm), and
two directly hired extension subject matter specialists (SMS) in the field support staff at Quetta; six AOs
stationed throughout the field offices; some 50 DOA FAs located in or near scheme areas; and some 45-
60 VDAs, one per FO. The internationally recruited agricultural economist located with PDS would
assist the team with advice on crop gross margins, crop prices and marketing data, profitability of current
and potential cropping patterns and other matters. Immediately at scheme screening and detailed design
the agricultural staff (particularly the extension specialist, agricultural economist, SMS and AOs) would
meet and discuss with farmers potential best use of additional water, and possible impacts this might have
on scheme design. This would, of course, be continued during the first year of aftercare when choice
of cropping pattern could strongly dictate CAD works required. Once FO members have decided their
cropping patterns, the team would advise them on crop varieties, best appropriate production methods,



- 40 -
harvesting, marketing and storage. The team would stage field days and demonstrations, undertake
appropriate on-farm research, and produce suitable extension leaflets, videos or other material. The
agricultural extension expert, along with SMSs, would lead, plan and supervise the team's program.
After scheme construction, AOs, assisted by FAs, would regularly visit FOs at least monthly and give
advice and help and arrange training concerning farmer's priority problems. The team would encourage
each FO to nominate a person from their community to be trained to work as a first level extension agent
or VDA. VDAs would be the team's main contact with FOs, and should have a positive personality, be
respected in the community and be a farmer although in some cases a local school teacher or such person
may be suitable. If possible, FO members should remunerate VDA for his services. As stated, BRSP
would give VDA their initial training, while the project would provide considerable in-service training
for AOs and other team members.
C. Monitoring, Evaluation and Supervision
4.10       Monitoring. Monitoring of the project would be "management oriented", that is, designed
to provide information to assist internal project management. PMU's MIS section would monitor and
analyze project activities at four levels, that is, project inputs (number of staff hired, vehicles, material
procured and so on); outputs (headworks, canals, CAD works constructed); effects or success in meeting
project objectives (use easily identifiable and measurable indicators such as crop yields or increased
orchard or other crops planted); and long-term project impacts such as increased farmer incomes. The
internationally recruited monitoring and evaluation (M&E) specialist (7 sm) would assist MIS section,
and overall project staff, design and implement appropriate monitoring systems. This would require a
meeting of all project management staff to decide what should be monitored, what indicators used, who
collects information and who receives, and to set annual work targets. MIS should monitor and analyze
long-term project impacts through case studies, not large project-wide surveys. MIS would have a
complaints officer to whom FO or community members could bring complaints concerning project
implementation, and have them dealt with in a transparent manner. IDA obtained assurances from GOBal
that PMU, in accordance with guidelines and on the basis of performance indicators agreed with IDA,
would adequately monitor project implementation, and evaluate the achievement of project objectives.
4.11       Project Launch Workshop. To facilitate project start-up, IDA along with BID and PMU
would organize and hold a project launch workshop soon after credit signing. The workshop would
familiarize implementation staff with project objectives, components and other administrative aspects.
4.12       Evaluation.  BID, DOA, IDA (with FAO Country Program staff), BRSP and other
appropriate NGOs would undertake two interim evaluations of the project, the first no later than
September 30, 1997, and the second by September 30, 1999. IDA obtained assurances from GOBal that
BID and DOA, in accordance with terms of reference acceptable to IDA, and jointly with IDA, would
undertake two interim evaluations of the project, the first by September 30, 1997, and the second by
September 30, 1999, and would produce reports identifying any required changes in project
implementation with which project implementing staff would comply.
4.13       IDA Supervision. While IDA would supervise the project at more than average rates
during the initial year, from then on IDA would use normal supervision inputs. This higher rate of
supervision during project start-up is justified because of the project's importance in initiating GOBal and
IDA's new irrigation development strategy. Annex 7 contains IDA's proposed supervision plan, and
details the required annual staff inputs and expected skill requirements needed on supervision missions.



- 41 -
The project would provide PMU 20 smLR consultants to assist with supervision and evaluation, and 20
smLR consultants to help prepare a future community irrigation project. During the project's first full
implementation year (July 1995 to June 1996), IDA would use approximately 21 sw for all supervision
activities, 16 sw in the second year, and about 14-15 sw for each of the remaining years. During
supervision IDA staff should pay particular attention to PMU moving quickly to complete all scheme
screening and detailed design work, procuring vehicles, equipment and civil works contractors, forming
and strengthening FOs, and supplying FOs water management, agricultural extension, and irrigation
construction and O&M training services. Annex 7 also contains key indicators of project implementation
which supervision missions and PMU staff should monitor.
V. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION, MARKET PROSPECTS,
AND FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Agricultural Production
5.1        The project would increase agricultural production in Balochistan through augmented water
supplies to some existing irrigated areas, which would help alleviate deficit crop water requirements, and
through an expansion of cropped irrigated area. The scale of this incremental production resulting from
developing the 30 minor irrigation schemes was estimated by modelling the production and cropping
patterns of a set of representative irrigation schemes based on several schemes recently screened or
prepared to detailed design during project preparation.
5.2        On the basis of the modelling exercise (see Implementation Volume, Project Costing and
Analysis annex), the project is expected to increase the cropped area under minor irrigation by less than
1,000 ha. The expectation, or target, may be considered to be about 920 ha over the five full years of
the project's implementation. Of this area, about 830 ha would be planted in newly established deciduous
fruit orchards: 150 ha of mixed orchards, about 510 ha of traditional apple varieties and about 170 ha
of semi-dwarf apples. The remaining incremental irrigated areas would be planted in arable food and
fodder crops. Together with existing irrigated areas (some 2,140 ha), on which some changes in cropping
patterns are expected to combine with yield improvements, the project at full development should to lead
to net production increases around 550 tons of fodder, 6,450 tons of arable crops, 3,270 tons of mixed
orchard fruits and 14,200 tons of apples. Table 5.1 and Annex 8, Tables 2-6 and 14 show assumed crop
yields, cropping patterns for farms at different altitudes, and overall project cropping pattern.
Agricultural production from the development of the mini and flood schemes may increase these
production estimates by about 20 percent (approximately 500 ha under mini schemes and 1,200 ha under
flood schemes).
B. Marketing Prospects
5.3        Despite the difficult terrain of the province, marketing networks within Balochistan are
surprisingly extensive. It is also a rather sophisticated private-sector operation where purchasing agents,
truckers, wholesalers and other middlemen provide a range of fairly well defined differentiated services.
State intervention in marketing is limited to the subsidization of some staples and of some agricultural
inputs and to regulating the activities of commission agents, but in general the markets are largely free
and unregulated. There is also a notable movement of produce into and out of Afghanistan and Iran.
Balochistan is a net importer of food.



- 42 -
5.4         The main marketing policy of the provincial government has centered on the ADB-financed
Fruit and Vegetable Marketing Project, which is a national project. Project objectives are to establish
an organized fruit and vegetable market, regulate the activities of commission agents through licenses,
improve marketing information and undertake research on post harvest technology. The project includes
the construction of a new wholesale market in Quetta.
5.5        The project also prompted the introduction of a revised Balochistan Agricultural Produce
Markets Act 1991, which replaces the Agricultural Produce Act 1939. The Agricultural Produce Markets
Act provides for the regulation of the purchase and sale of agricultural produce and the establishment of
markets for agricultural produce in the province. The act aims to improve the administration of local
markets in line with other provinces, notably Punjab, and allows the government to exercise limited
control over the purchase and sale of produce in a notified area through the issuance of licenses for
commission agents, who then come under the supervision of a local marketing committee.
5.6        Actual demand for good quality fresh produce is limited within Balochistan, except for
Quetta. Much of the province's fresh produce is sent on to main markets in Sindh and Punjab. The
major destinations for Balochistan produce are Faisalabad, Multan, Lahore and Karachi. Dera Ghazi
Khan is an important transfer market for produce leaving the area. Transport is normally plentiful and
prices are market driven.
5.7        There are normally few storage facilities at the market level. Most produce is held for a
limited time by the commission agent and at wholesale markets. The province lacks cold storage facilities
for fruits; although, the ADB Fruit and Vegetable Marketing Project may help ease this constraint with
the construction of cold storage facilities at Quetta and Lorali. The wholesale marketing of goods is well
structured with specialization in the various commodities. The wholesale operation attracts a degree of
risk as the wholesalers buy produce often on credit from the commission agent.
5.8        The marketing of fruits is conducted mostly on the basis of contracts to reduce the risk to
the farmer and to compensate for limited access to market information, which is a constraint for the more
remote areas of the province. The contractor would advance a portion of the expected income from the
crop, which might amount to as much as 50%. This arrangement is normally entered into early in the
season, and so the contractor in principle bears the risk of any loss or downward fluctuation in price.
In some arrangements, the contractor would also be responsible for crop protection measures until
harvest. The contractor is normally responsible for labor required for harvesting and for all packaging
and transportation to the market.
5.9        Direct marketing by farmers is becoming increasingly popular, partly through improvements
in rural telecommunications. The proportion of farmers taking their own produce to market is notably
on the rise in the main apple areas around Zhob, Lorali and Ziarat. The marketing of vegetables and
melons is similar to the marketing of fruit. In general, however, contract harvesters are not a prominent
feature; although in the major vegetable growing areas such as Harnai, contractors do operate. Also,
compared to the marketing of fruit, a higher proportion of farmers market their vegetables themselves.
This is particularly characteristic of onion producers, who are often prepared to travel from Nushki, close
to the border with Iran and Afghanistan, to Lahore if they have a good crop.



- 43 -
5.10       The growing importance of urban markets in the province is apparent with the specialization
of some farmers, particularly in the Pishin district, who produce early tomatoes and other early crops
through the growing of seedlings in Sukkur or through the use of plastic green houses.
5.11       The bulk of the grain grown in Balochistan is kept for home consumption. In some areas,
however, where sailaba and rain-fed agriculture dominate, wheat is a significant cash crop. The market
price is depressed by the government's continuing subsidization of wheat consumption.
5.12       Fodder crops are grown throughout the province, both in perennial and sailaba irrigated
land. They provide an important cash crop and are critical for animal husbandry. The market for fodder
is substantial in urban areas as a result of dairies and the wide-spread use of draft animals. In particular,
there is a substantial market for fodder in the Sibi area and in the Kachhi plains in general. Breeding
dairy cows and draft bullocks is an important source of income here. Sibi also lies on the traditional
route of nomads who pass from the winter grazing in the Kachhi Plains to the summer grazing in the
hills. They frequently buy fodder to supplement the range grazing. Fodder is handled separately from
the main fruit and vegetable marketing. Some fodder is grown on a contract basis. This fodder (wheat,
barley or oats) is then frequently taken to Quetta. Prices for fodder in the main markets remain fairly
constant throughout the year, but may seasonally fluctuate in local markets.
5.13       Balochistan has some minor crops, such as cherries and cumin, which will probably see
local expansion. The demand for cherries is increasing with the market and wholesale price rising
steeply. In the higher areas, farmers are experimenting with cherries and have planted some substantial
areas to the crop around Ziarat. As the crop attracts a high price, it is normally marketed directly by
the farmer. The growing of cumin, although not widespread, does form an important income to farmers
in areas without much water and may often be their only cash crop.
5.14       In summary, no major difficulties in the marketing of the project's incremental production
are foreseen. The markets for apples and other fruits are expected to continue to expand in Karachi and
Sindh. The same is expected to be true for the marketing of vegetables. Balochistan has a comparative
advantage in providing vegetables off season in other provinces. Moreover, the project's production is
not expected to be on a scale to have much of an impact on market prices, either within the province or
in its main markets outside.
C. Financial Analysis
Prices
5.15       For the financial and economic analyses, inputs and outputs have been valued at present and
projected (2000 - 2005) prices expressed in March 1995 constant values. The economic analysis
estimates the incremental benefits of scheme developments in terms of production-efficiency measures
of opportunity costs to the economy, and uses border prices, at March 1995 value, for major tradeable
commodities derived from World Bank projections of cif or fob values adjusted for local handling and
transportation costs to the farm gate. The derivation of border prices for major agricultural commodities
was based partially on data presented in "Pakistan: Irrigation and Drainage Sector Strategy Review" and
adjusted for current prices. and data collected during appraisal. The commodity price projections used
were those provided in the World Bank's "Revision of Primary Commodity Price Forecasts and Quarterly
Review of Commodity Markets, May 1994". In general, the crop and activities budgets of the analysis



- 44 -
use the average of prices expected to prevail over the period between 1995 and 2005. The border prices
for non-tradeable commodities that constitute minor project costs were estimated from current local prices
using a Standard Conversion Factor (SCF) of 0.9. The SCF was also used for minor costs of tradeable
commodities. The financial wage for casual labor in rural Balochistan was priced at R 56/day, which
was considered to be a reasonable estimate of marginal value product. Annex 8, Tables 13a and b show
the financial and economic prices used in the financial and economic analysis.
5.16          Crop Budgets and Yields. There are financial and economic versions of crop budgets,
and of scheme and project models of the minor irrigation development.  The financial version
incorporates proposed household cash and labor contributions, project provisions for the scheme
investments and household repayments of scheme capital costs in generating net cash flows before and
after financing. The economic version proceeds on the basis of economic values of production and inputs
as mentioned earlier.
5.17       In the future without the project, the analysis assumed that productivity of existing
agriculture would improve over time. Generally, this was reflected in an increase in yields of from 0.5-
1 % a year over the next twenty years. In some cases, future without and future with the project yields
are close twenty or twenty-five years from now, but with the project's extension services delivered
through its field offices higher production is achieved much sooner than in the future without the project.
Table 5.1 summarizes the yield increases assumed in the analysis. In the analysis, the high altitude and
mid-altitude irrigation schemes share some horticultural crop models. Some of the highest yield increases
are expected to occur in the low altitude areas, where the marginal benefit of additional water for severely
stressed crops is considered high. The geographic location of the these schemes, however, will also
strongly determine whether communities will actually benefit from the production potential. Note also
that semi-dwarf apples start to bear fruit in the fourth year of planting, whereas new traditional apples
and mixed orchards begin to bear in year eight of planting. All yields are mission estimates based on
field observations and data collected under BMIAD and during project preparation. Details of the
production, yields and budgets of the crop models are presented in tables attached to the project working
paper on Project Costing and Analysis, June 1994.
5.18       The analysis defined five models of minor perennial irrigation schemes:
High Altitude -- Area Expansion
Mid-Altitude -- Constant Area
Mid-Altitude -- Area Expansion
Low Altitude -- Constant Area
Low Altitude -- Area Expansion
The second and fourth scheme models involve the addition of water on existing cropped areas. Yields
improve as crops are less stressed and changes in agronomic practices are introduced through project field
services. The remaining models anticipate some expansion of cropped areas. In each of the schemes
there is some modification in cropping patterns following the introduction of additional flows of water.
Annex 8, Table 14 shows details of total cropping pattern changes.

Farm Incomes
5.19           Impact on Farm  Incomes. Average farm  incomes from  irrigated agriculture are expected
to increase by about Rs 11,000 to Rs 20,000 in the medium  run and by Rs 11,000 to 65,000 in the long
run, measured in March 1995 prices. The "medium run" refers to years eight to ten in a scheme's
development. The "long run" is 17 to 20 years into a scheme's development. The greatest change in
the long run is expected to occur for mid-altitude schemes, where the dominant cropping pattern shifts
from wheat production to horticulture. In the medium run, however, the largest gains in incomes should
be seen among the low altitude schemes specializing in off-season vegetable production.
5.20           The estimates of farm  income were based on the following household holdings of irrigated
land. Sizes vary by scheme and whether additional land is brought under command through the project.
The last scheme illustrates the case of villages such as Kunara, where irrigation is at present extremely
limited, but can be -- although not cheaply -- much expanded.

Financial Rates of Return
5.23             Scheme Financing.  In the financial high and mid-altitude scheme budgets of the analysis,
households contribute 9 % of the estimated capital costs of main works in cash in the first year of scheme
construction. Households also contribute 6% of the cost of the main works in unskilled labor. The
number of days this represents was calculated using the rate charged for contracted hires (Rs 78/day).
The project, however, will pay the contractor's margin to households for their days of contributed labor.
This margin was estimated at Rs 22/day. Households are also expected to repay 15% of the cost of the
main works, in real terms. This appears in the budgets in the form of long-term credit that is repaid at
12% interest over 6 years, including one year of grace. The project would carry the remaining capital
cost of the main works of each scheme. In the scheme budgets this residual financing appears as part
of a grant to households.



- 48 -
5.24         For the households in the low  altitude areas, scheme financing reflected these areas'
relatively poor socio-economic conditions. Here, the average cash contribution is expected to be 1 % of
the capital cost of main works. The unskilled labor contribution is 6% of main works investment costs,
while 8% in real terms will be repaid to BID over five years, including one year of grace.
5.25         In the analysis, FOs are responsible for the financing, in cash and in kind, of CAD works,
which are estimated at 2.5 percent of the investment costs of main works. The operation and maintenance
of the schemes are solely the responsibility of the participating households. Where scheme investments
enable expansions of cropped areas, households will also be liable for the costs of developing and
preparing incremental areas of land for agriculture. The analysis used the distribution of schemes as
shown in Table 5. 1.
5.26         Financial Results for Schemes. Table 5.5 gives the financial rates of return for farmers
with an average area of irrigated land in the various cropping systems. The analysis shows two rates of
return, the first and smallest when the farmer has to meet all his development costs, the second when the
farmer meets 15 or 30% of the real capital costs of the scheme as proposed under this project. These
second financial rates of return, varying from 34 to 99%, should be attractive to farmers. Significant
demands are made on household labor not only for their portion of investment costs, but also for the
construction of CAD works and, in the cases where cropped area expands, land development.
