VI. BENEFITS, ECONOMIC ANALYSIS, ENVIRONMENTAL EFFECTS AND RISKS
A. Benefits and Economic Analysis
Benefits
6.1        The main project benefits center around institutional developments and policy improvements
in Balochistan's irrigation and drainage sector. The project would develop sustainable FOs which could
play an effective role in planning, implementing and operating minor irrigation development. It would
change and strength BID's capabilities so it can better interact with communities, provide them more
relevant services, and assist them to eventually take full responsibility for developing their minor
irrigation resources. The project would ensure that project beneficiaries paid 100% of the irrigation
systems O&M charges, and introduce the concept of irrigators paying for the capital costs of schemes.
The project would initiate a program which could lead to the beneficiaries of all minor irrigation schemes
in Balochistan paying 100% of O&M charges. Under the project, and according to their ability to pay,
FO members would contribute either 15 or 30% of the scheme's capital costs. This level of capital costs
payment is the first step towards having all irrigators eventually contribute 100% of irrigation capital cost
development.
6.2        The project would increase crop yields and agricultural production on some 4,800 ha (see
para 5.2), benefiting approximately 7,200 farm families. During construction it would provide additional
employment opportunities for local contractors, and skilled and unskilled laborers. Through increasing
agricultural production, it would create an additional demand for local farm labor, and for transport and
other services.  Other smaller project benefits include improvements in health resulting from the
incidental provision of potable water supplies accompanying several of the schemes, improvements in
village amenities through providing clothes washing and animal drinking facilities in the irrigation channel
system, and some improvements in the status of rural women. Finally, the development of strong,
sustainable FOs could open further rural development opportunities, for example, these village
organizations could be used for other development programs, and they could give communities a stronger
voice in requesting government services or organizing better agricultural input supplies and/or commodity
marketing services.
6.3        Project Participants. Project participants would be small, remote farming communities.
Although those living above 1,300 m grow high value crops, the average irrigated area (0.5 ha) per farm
is much smaller than the average irrigated area (4.0 ha) per farm in Balochistan's Indus supplied
irrigation area. The general incidence of poverty throughout the project areas would be at least equivalent
to the general level in Balochistan's rural population (43%), while the incidence amongst project area
communities living below 1,300 m could be greater. Scheme selection criteria would ensure that only
communities with generally equitable water and land right distributions would be accepted into project
activities. Communities are expected to distribute additional water rights resulting from the project
roughly according to existing water rights.
Economic Analysis
6.4        Quantified Benefits. Because of the difficulties of quantifying the project's institutional
and policy improvement benefits, the economic analysis only considered the incremental agricultural
benefits resulting from project investments. Additionally, the analysis only included agricultural benefits



- 51 -
from the 30 minor irrigation schemes. It excluded the mini and flood irrigation schemes as the mini
schemes are primarily to provide training opportunities for BID staff, and the flood schemes are a pilot
program for which the benefits have yet to be adequately determined.
6.5        Incremental agricultural benefits are derived from modelling the production and cropping
patterns of a set of representative irrigation schemes that are based on several recently screened schemes,
and on schemes now prepared to detail design. Chapter V, paras 15-18, and Table 5.1 specify or discuss:
(a) the five minor irrigation scheme models considered; (b) expected future crop yields with and without
the project; and (c) economic prices for farm inputs and outputs. The economic wage for casual labor
in Balochistan was derived from the financial wage (see para 5.15) by using a SCF of 0.9. Annex 8,
Table 14 shows details of total cropping pattern changes in the without and with project situations, while
the project working paper Project Costing and Analysis, June 1994 (project files) gives crop budgets and
net agricultural benefit cash flows for each minor irrigation scheme model.
6.6        Investment Costs. Investment costs for the economic analysis include the construction costs
of the 30 minor irrigation schemes, 70% of the project's total engineering, administrative and aftercare
period costs, and 50% of the community development and support services which PMU would provide.
Investment costs also include CAD development costs and all necessary farm development costs such as
land clearing, earth moving and land levelling. Costs include physical contingencies, but exclude price
contingencies. To convert the financial cost values to economic values (that is, correct for taxes,
subsidies and other transfer payments), the various financial investment costs were multiplied by
individual conversion factors ranging from 0.82 to 0.88. Annual scheme O&M costs have been added
to the investment cost cash flows.
6.7        Environmental Externalities. As paras 6.14-17 state, the project should not have any large
adverse environment effects. Consequently, it is not necessary to adjust economic net benefits to account
for the effects of environmental externalities.
6.8        Development Period.  Following the completion of minor irrigation scheme design,
construction would take around 18 months with another 18 months required to design and construct CAD
works. Some additional water would be available in year three of construction, but generally full supplies
would become available in year four. For high and mid-altitude scheme models, benefits would peak
around year 15 (mainly orchard crops grown), while for low altitude models, benefits peak around years
seven/eight (only annual crops grown).
6.9        Economic Rates of Return. Using the forgoing assumptions and discounting project costs
and benefits over 25 years, the project's ERR for the minor schemes as a whole is estimated at 14%.
While this may not be a particularly high ERR, it is acceptable given the high costs of working with
remote and scattered communities in Balochistan, and as the sensitivity analysis shows, it appears robust.
ERRs for the various irrigation scheme models vary from 6 to 15% (see Table 6.1). Assuming an
opportunity cost of capital of 10%, except for the low altitude constant area model, these ERRs are
satisfactory. Although this model is expected to have a large proportion of farmers below the poverty
line, any scheme with an ERR below 10% should be rejected. Before rejecting a scheme, staff should
analyze alternative project designs and operating methods.  Because the analyses were unable to
incorporate the main project institutional and policy development benefits, it is expected that any scheme
with a calculated 10% ERR would have a much higher actual ERR. Deciduous fruit production
contributes substantially to the performance of the high and mid-altitude schemes. The performance of



- 52 -
low altitude schemes relies on the production of vegetables. The returns to the low-altitude area
expansion scheme stem from a significant expansion of cropped area with the project. The high and mid-
altitude cropping systems generally are likely to be the more productive as here farmers have excellent
opportunities to grow high value crops. The low-altitude farmers are more dependent on finding a special
market niche such as early or late season vegetables or mellons.

6.10         Sensitivity Analysis. The economic rates of return are reasonably insensitive to delays in
benefit flows, increases in project costs, and decreases in expected incremental production benefits.
Potential project risks such as ineffective, unsustainable FOs, tribal disputes, weak contractors and a too
defuse geographical spread of schemes are most likely to adversely affect the project by lengthening the
construction period or increasing construction costs. Of these risks, tribal disputes and weak contractors
are most likely to impinge on the project, and their main effect would be to lengthen the construction
period. If overall project construction was lengthened by one or two years (that is, benefits lagged by
one or two years), then ERR would fall to 13 % or 11 % respectively. While the probability of a one year
delay is quite high, say around 40%, the probability of a two year delay is most likely closer to 20%,
or less. With regard to construction costs, any increases are likely to fall within a range of 10-15%. As
Table 6.2 shows, this would not depress ERR to an unacceptable level.
6.11         Switching Values. Table 6.2 shows the loci of ERR switching values, assuming 10 and
12% opportunity costs of capital. Costs could increase by 40% or benefits decrease by 29% before ERR
would fall below 10%, or costs could increase by about 22% or benefits decrease by some 20% before
ERR would fall below 12%. As already mentioned (para 6.10), possible cost increases are expected to
be below 20%. It is of interest that when project costs are disaggregated, to lower ERR to 10%
individual costs could increase as follows: (a) scheme investment costs could rise by 90%; (b) cropping
costs by 145 %; (c) investment costs and cropping costs together by 55 %; and (d) engineering, community
support and project administration together by 140%.

6.12       Benefits could be adversely affected by lower-value cropping patterns, lower crop yields and
poor irrigation maintenance. The evaluation assumed quite conservative cropping patterns (in production
value) and crop yields, plus the project does much to ensure good O&M of the systems, thus the
probability of obtaining expected project benefits is high. The possibility of benefits falling by more than
15% is considered to be quite low. Thus the overall project investments in minor irrigation can be
considered acceptable at both a 10 and 12% opportunity cost of capital.
6.13       Project Fiscal Impact.  While in the short-run this project would add to GOBal's
development expenditures on irrigation, in the long-run it should lead to a reduction in both GOBal's
development and recurrent expenditures on irrigation.  Under the project, FO members would be
responsible for 100% of irrigation scheme O&M costs, and would contribute/repay 15 or 30% of the
scheme capital costs. The project's pilot program to transfer O&M responsibilities for all minor
irrigation within Balochistan to FOs could eventually lead to a reduction in BID's recurrent expenditures
on O&M. As well, this project initiates GOBal's policy to eventually recover 100% of irrigation capital
costs from irrigators. This policy would eventually considerably reduce the amount government funds
expended on irrigation development. Additionally, FO members would pay local taxes on their
incremental agricultural production.
B. Environmental Effects
6. 14      According to the provisions of the Bank-Group Operational Directive 4.01 (Environmental
Assessment), this project belongs in Category B as regards the nature, magnitude and sensitivity of its
possible environmental effects. The following paragraphs list the results of an environmental review''
to assess the significance of possible environmental effects resulting from project activities, and to
determine measures to enhance environmental management in the project areas. Project staff will try to
identify potential environmental problems during scheme planning, and design appropriate measures to
mitigate their effects.  If this is not possible, staff will reject any schemes where they consider
environmental negative effects outweigh project benefits. Staff will monitor project environmental effects.
6.15       The project would have a beneficial environmental impact on communities health where it
provides potable water supplies. Additionally, project staff would train communities in the correct use
and handling of potable water so they do not contaminate it. Where irrigation canals pass through
villages, staff would consider using pipes to convey the water so villagers do not dump household effluent
into it and cause a serious health hazard for farmers, and children who play in the water. While malaria
is endemic in Balochistan, to guard against existing or additional water supplies creating breeding habitats
for mosquitos which carry the disease, the project would design and use canal structures which are free
draining.
6.16       The use of fertilizers in Balochistan (50 kg/ha) on irrigated land is low compared to Europe
(400 kg/ha). Even allowing an increase in fertilizer use under the project, the risk of nitrates being
leached into groundwater is remote. However, while pesticide use is also low in Balochistan, project
farmers are likely to increase their use of them (particularly on orchards), and this poses real risks to both
farmer's health through lax handling and spraying procedures, and the public's health through excessive
Environmental Impact and Use of Agro-Chemicals, Project Preparation
Report, BMIAD-II, Volume 6, Annex XIX



- 55 -
dangerous residues either in or on vegetables and fruit. To minimize these risks, the project extension
service would put considerable efforts into training farmers in the correct handling and spraying of
pesticides, promoting the use of the least-dangerous chemicals, and minimizing the number of spraying.
To the extent possible, extension staff would design and promote integrated pest management programs
for the protection of horticultural, and if necessary, other crops.
6.17        As the project will not finance tubewells, it will not contribute to lowering of groundwater
levels. As well, under the project farmers generally are not expected to dig wells to augment their main
water supply. Where the project improves diversion works, staff will be careful to maintain traditional
water rights between different groups or schemes. Staff will also ensure that scheme designs include
adequate drainage, flood protection and soil erosion works.
C. Risks
6.18        The key issue in the sustainability of the development of the schemes is the role and
performance of the FO. If an FO was to operate ineffectively or fail, the implementation of its scheme
would likely collapse or deteriorate rapidly for lack of proper routine maintenance. The most likely
reasons for FOs failing include traditional leaders perceiving them or WGs threatening their authority,
FO office-bearers not fully understanding their roles, and inadequate incentives to keep FO members
together. Project staff would need to develop mechanisms for dialogue and consultation between
traditional village leaders and FOs and WGs, and would, to the extent possible, involve them in the
leadership of these groups. The project's community support services are strongly oriented towards
minimizing the possibilities of these issues being destabilizing to FOs or WGs.
6.19        Scheme location and distribution are critical to the overall economic justification of the
project. Potentially, there may be several low altitude areas where cropped area can be expanded, but
per hectare, returns are higher for higher altitude schemes. If a larger proportion of low altitude schemes
than presently proposed are built, the project might be much less economically desirable. The size and
proportion of small schemes to large ones also has an expected bearing on the overall economic
performance of the project.
6.20        The selection of suitable flood schemes is particularly difficult due to the complex sociology
of the areas. Also, in these areas there are particular difficulties in estimating water flow and the market
linkages, if not already present on a rudimentary scale, may be slow to develop. The return on the
investment in the low altitude and flood scheme areas can be rather respectable since production in these
areas would be improving from a very low base. The success of the schemes, however, would depend
on the ability of the project to pick schemes with a high annual probability of useable flood flows and
low unit cost of development, and provide the required organizational building, extension assistance and
engineering services as needed, and the willingness of farmers to accept the improvements afforded by
available technologies.
6.21        The geographic spread of the schemes potentially under consideration may complicate
project management and add to costs. To some extent, however, these difficulties have been anticipated;
the establishment of several field offices under the project should help mitigate logistical difficulties. As
well, the project would give preference to suitable schemes which are close to each other, and within
reasonable distance of the field offices.



- 56 -
6.22       The performance of contractors, which was a major constraint to the efficient, early
implementation of the BMIADP, could also be a cause for concern. Strict post qualification and selection
procedures that have been established in the later stages of BMIADP, along with rigorous monitoring
have resulted in a small, but adequate pool of reasonably capable contractors. The risk of poor contractor
performance under the present project still exists, but is considered now to have been much reduced.
6.23       FO members may not repay their share of irrigation infrastructure capital costs. Project
design, however, is oriented towards encouraging farmers to meet this obligation. Firstly, the project
would not accept a scheme for construction until FO members agreed to the repayment of capital costs
as delineated in the scheme agreement. FO members would then pay around 50% of their share of capital
costs during scheme design and construction. Payments would be linked to specific milestones in the
planning and construction process so that project management could halt development should FO members
fail to make the necessary payments. After scheme construction, FOs would collect their members'
remaining repayments over as short a period as possible, and could bring peer pressure on any defaulters.
The local government administration, in agreement with FOs and the local BID Executive Engineer,
would use FO repayments for high priority local development expenditures. Thus the use of these funds
would remain within the local communities. Finally, GOBal would only transfer ownership of any newly
constructed infrastructure to FOs when their members have fully contributed their share of capital costs.
VII. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.1        During negotiations IDA agreed with GOBal on the following points:
(a)   by December 31, 1995 PMU would recruit and place all its higher level (BPS-16 and
above) seconded staff, that is, from BID a Project Director (BPS-20), Assistant
Project Director (BPS-19), Assistant Director Finance (BPS-18), four Area Engineers
(BPS-18), and four Junior Engineers (BPS-17), and from DOA six Agricultural
Officers (BPS-17) (para 3.22);
(b)   PMU would: (i) by January 31, 1996 furnish proposals for any NGO-implemented
training under the project to IDA for its prior review and approval; (ii) by April 30,
1996, under conditions acceptable to IDA, would sign a memorandum of
understanding with an appropriate NGO to supply training services; and (iii) furnish
proposals for other training under the project to IDA for its prior review and
approval (para 3.28);
(c)   PMU would locally recruit consultants or consultant firms, with qualifications and
under conditions acceptable to IDA, to supply the services which para 3.29(c)(vi)
lists (para 3.30);
(d)   BID would: (i) maintain identifiable project accounts which independent auditors,
acceptable to IDA and using procedures satisfactory to IDA, would audit annually;
(ii) have its auditors issue separate opinions on the project accounts, disbursements
from the special account, and SOEs and other supporting documents used as a basis
for disbursements from the credit; (iii) provide its auditors unaudited project accounts
within three months of the fiscal years' end (by September 30); and (iv) submit an



- 57 -
audited report of project accounts to IDA within nine months of the fiscal years' end
(by March 31) (para 3.45);
(e)   PMU would: (i) within six months of credit completion prepare, under guidelines
acceptable to IDA, a plan for the future operation of the project; (ii) afford IDA the
opportunity to exchange views with GOBal on the plan; and (iii) implement the plan
with due diligence and efficiency, taking into account IDA's comments on
implementation (para 4.3);
(f)   before proceeding to implement any irrigation scheme, the Project Director and FO,
in the presence of the Deputy Commissioner or other appropriate official, would sign
an agreement, which is acceptable to IDA, and which would describe both parties'
obligations and responsibilities under the project (para 4.5);
(g)   PMU, in consultation and agreement with FOs, shall identify, plan and prepare
project scheme proposals in a manner and in a form satisfactory to IDA; and when
an FO successfully completes its agreed contributions towards scheme capital costs,
BID would transfer to it full ownership rights over the improved headworks and
distribution system (para 4.6);
(h)   PMU, in accordance with guidelines and on the basis of performance indicators
agreed with IDA, would adequately monitor project implementation, and evaluate the
achievement of project objectives (para 4.10); and
(i)   BID and DOA, in accordance with terms of reference acceptable to IDA, and jointly
with IDA, would undertake two interim evaluations of the project, the first by
September 30, 1997, and the second by September 30, 1999, and would produce
reports identifying any required changes in project implementation with which project
implementing staff would comply (para 4.12).
7.2        The IDA credit would not become effective until:
(a)   PMU has signed a contract with an internationally recruited firm, or consortium of
firms, with qualifications and under conditions acceptable to IDA, to supply the
services which para 3.29(a)(i-v), (b) and (c)(i-v) lists, and the consultants are on
location ready to commence work (para 3.30);
(b)   ECNEC has fully approved the project's PC-I and sanctioned project expenditures
(para 3.34); and
(c)   all conditions precedent to the effectiveness of GON's Grant Agreement have been
fulfilled, other than effectiveness of IDA's credit agreement (para 3.34).
7.3        With the above assurances and conditions, the project would be suitable for an IDA credit
of SDR 18.5 million (US$26.7 million equivalent) on standard terms with a 35 year maturity. The
Borrower would be the Islamic Republic of Pakistan.

BALOCMHSTAN COMMUNITY IRRIGATION AND AGRICULTURE PROJECT
Draft Scheme Agreement"
This AGREEMENT is made the .......... day of ...  1995 between the
Balochistan Community Irrigated Agriculture Project of Jullandur House, Madressa Road, Quetta
Cantonment, Balochistan (hereinafter called the "Project") and
...................... Farmer Organization of ................ village ... District, Balochistan
(hereinafter called the "Farmer Organization")
and witnessed by the Deputy Commissioner .District, Balochistan (hereinafter called
the "Deputy Commissioner").
WHEREAS:
(a)    The Project is satisfied that the .......................... Perennial Irrigation Scheme confonns
to the Project selection criteria and the development proposals have been approved by the
Govemment of Balochistan and the Intemational Development Association, for
implementation under the Project.
(b)    The farmers have formned a Farmer Organization which has been registered under the
appropriate legislation, have opened a bank account for the sole and specific use of the
Farner Organization and have deposited the required amount in the account.
III    NOW IT IS HEREBY AGREED AS FOLLOWS:
Article 1     The Project will, subject to the Conditions of Agreement, undertake the planning,
design and implementation of the construction works and provide training and
support to the Farmer Organization.
Article 2     The Farmer Organization will, subject to the Conditions of Agreement, fully
participate in the development process, including undertaking the farnner
This draft scheme agreement is reproduced only to show what such an agreement would
contain. The project preparation consultants are currently revising this draft to incorporate
the levels of beneficiary contributions towards irrigation infrastructure capital costs agreed
at credit negotiations.

Page 2
construction component and making the necessary financial contributions to the
scheme funds.
Article 3            The  Farmer Organization  will take  full responsibility  for the  operation  and
maintenance of the irrigation scheme in a correct and satisfactory manner.
Article 4            The Deputy Commissioner will, subject to the Conditions of Agreement, act as
arbitrator in resolving disputes and in the interpretation of this Agreement and his
decision will be final and binding. As witness to this Agreement, the Deputy
Commissioner confirms acceptance of this role.
Article 5            The following documents jointly form this Scheme Agreement:
(i)                  Agreement
(ii)                 Conditions of Agreement
(iii)                Appendix A                     Statement by members of the Farner
Organization confirming the powers of
the elected Committee
(iv)                 Appendix B                     Letter  establishing   consignatary   bank
account and agreeing to forfeiture of
monies in event of the non-perfornance
(v)                  Appendix C                     Financing schedule

I WHEREAS:
(a)    The Project is satisfied that the ...................... Perennial Irrigation Scheme conforms to
the Project selection criteria and the development proposals have been approved by the
Govemment of Balochistan and the International Development Association, for
implementation under the Project.
(b)    The farmers have formed a Farmer Organization which has been registered under the
appropriate legislation, have opened a bank account for the sole and specific use of the
Farmer Organization and have deposited the required amount in the account.
III    NOW IT IS HEREBY AGREED AS FOLLOWS:
Article I     The Project will, subject to the Conditions of Agreement, undertake the planning,
design and implementation of the construction works and provide training and
support to the Farmer Organization.
Article 2     The Farmer Organization will, subject to the Conditions of Agreement, fully
participate in the development process, including undertaking the farmer
construction component and making the necessary financial contributions to the
scheme funds.
Article 3     The Farmer Organization will take full responsibility for the operation and
maintenance of the irrigation scheme in a correct and satisfactory manner.

arbitrator in resolving disputes and in the interpretation of this Agreement and his
decision will be final and binding. As witness to this Agreement, the Deputy
Commissioner confirms acceptance of this role.

Conditions of Agreement
I     Definitions
Definitions          1.1   "Construction works" are defined as the physical infrastructure being
constructed by the Project.
1.2   "Development process" refers to the complete development package from
planning and design, through construction to extension and training.
1.3   "Main contract" means the component of the construction works to be
undertaken by a contractor.
1 .4   "Farmer contract" means the discrete component of the construction works
to be undertaken by the Farmer Organization.
1.5   "Capital Cost" is the estimated cost of the construction works, based on
contractor rates and including an allowance for physical contingencies.
1.6   "Preparation Phase" includes all the planning and design activities prior
to the actual tendering of the main contract.
1.7   "Construction Phase" commences with the tendering of the main contract
and ends with the final inspection and formal handling over of the scheme and
responsibility for the operation and maintenance to the Farmer Organization.
1.8   "Production Phase" refers to the five year period after construction is
complete. During the first two years, the Project would offer a broad-based
extension and training program.
2.     Special Considerations
Ownership of System  2.1    The members of the Farmer Organization shall retain full individual
ownership rights over the water and the improved headworks and distribution
system shall be the property of the Farmer Organization.
Exemption from       2.2    Provided always that this Agreement is fully complied with, the
Abiana               members of the Farmer Organization shall be exempt from the payment of Abiana
in respect of land owned or operated by them within the command area of the
scheme.
3      Planning, Design and Construction
Planning and Design 3.1    The Project shall undertake all surveys and site investigations necessary
to prepare the detailed designs for the construction works.



-8 7-
ANNEX 6
Page 6
3.2    The Project shall discuss the layout of the irrigation system with the
Farmer Organization and wherever technically and financially feasible the
requirements of the Farmer Organization would be incorporated into the design
of the improved system.
Approval of Detailed 3.3    After preparation of the draft detailed design, representatives from the
Design               Project and members of the Farrner Organization shall review the design
proposals on the ground and agree the alignment of canals, position of all
structures, operating criteria of the distribution system and details of any ancillary
structures included in the construction works. Any agreed modifications shall be
incorporated into the final detailed design which the committee of the Farmer
Organization shall approve by signing the final version of the scheme layout
drawings.
Subsequent changes  3.4    Having approved the detailed design, if the Farmer Organization
to Detailed Design    subsequently request modifications, then the Project would consider such changes
only if they are technically viable and where they can be incorporated without
increasing the capital cost of the construction works. Where the changes would
increase the overall capital costs, then the Farmer Organization would have to
finance, in advance, the full cost of implementing such changes.
3.5    The Project shall inform the Farmer Organization in writing of any
modifications to the agreed layout of the irrigation system which may prove
necessary during construction due to any unforeseen conditions or obstructions
and obtain their written approval for such changes.
Construction         3.6    The Project shall implement the construction works using satisfactory
quality materials and workmanship to appropriate national and international
standards.
4      Farmer Organization
General Obligations  4.1    The Farmer Organization shall comply with the requirements of the
Balochistan Water Users Ordinance 1981 and any subsequent amendments or new
legislation which supersede this ordinance.
4.2    The members of the Farmer Organization shall elect a committee which
is empowered to act on their behalf. to sign legally binding documents and to
exercise the powers vested in it in the constitution of the organization.  A
statement to this effect, signed by all the members of the Farmer Organization is
given in Appendix A.
4.3    The Farmer Organization shall take full responsibility for the operation
and maintenance of the irrigation scheme after the final inspection and joint
approval of the completed construction works by the Project and the Farmer
Organization.



- 88 -
ANNEX 6
Page 7
4.4    The Farmer Organization are required to contribute towards the capital
cost of the construction works and make provision for the cost of the maintenance
of the infrastructure once the scheme is complete. Contributions are payable into
two separate funds; a Development Fund and a Maintenance Fund. In addition,
the Farmer Organization shall be required to contribute to the capital cost of the
construction works.
4.5    The Farner Organization shall grant full and free access to the site to the
contractor, sub-contractor and Project employees. Where this involves access
across property belonging to a third party, the Farmer Organization shall obtain
the necessary permission and approval from the landowner. The Project shall
after giving 28 days written notice, terrninate the development process if the
Farmer Organization fail to provide the necessary access.
4.6    The Farmer Organization shall compensate members and third parties for
loss or damage to standing crops, the acquisition of land and any other negative
impact resulting from the proposed development. The Farmer Organization shall
not be responsible for damage caused by negligence on the part of the contractor
or sub-contractor.
Nomination of        4.7    The Farmer Organization shall nominate a responsible individual who
Representative       shall act as the contact person between the organization and the Project.
Bank Accounts        4.8    The Farmer Organization  shall open separate accounts for the
Development Fund and Maintenance Fund at a bank approved by the Project.
The committee of the Farmer Organization shall provide the bank with a mandate
signed by all the members of the committee detailing the names of those persons
who are empowered to sign withdrawal notices and other documents on behalf of
the organization. Both accounts shall be in the name of the Farmer Organization
and shall be operated in accordance with the constitution of the organization. The
operation of the Development Fund account would be subject to a number of
conditions and the Farmer Organization Committee are required to sign the letter
addressed to the bank where the account is held, given in Appendix B,
authorizing them to establish the necessary procedures for compliance with the
conditions. A separate signed copy of the letter shall be sent direct to the bank.
Development Fund   4.9    The Farmer Organization shall agree to the Project being a cosignatory
on the Development Fund account, so that withdrawals, transfers or any other
transactions involving access to the funds would require the signature of either of
the two named representatives from the Project and the requisite number of
signatories required in the mandate described in Clause 4.8. The initial Rs 10,000
instalment shall be transferred to the Development Fund.

8     Settlement of Disputes
General Approach    8.1   If a dispute or difference of any kind whatsoever shall arise, every effort
shall be made to obtain an amicable settlement between the parties concerned
without involving a third party. where such a settlement cannot be reached the
use of a conciliator shall be considered.  As a final solution the Deputy
Commissioner shall be considered. As a final solution the Deputy Commissioner
shall be asked to act as arbitrator and his decision shall be binding on both
parties.
Internal Disputes    8.2   Internal disputes involving land and water rights or other matters directly
related to the irrigation scheme shall in the first instance be handled by Fanner
Organization with the possible introduction of a conciliator acceptable to both
parties. If the matter cannot be resolved, it shall be referred to the Deputy
Commissioner and his decision shall be considered final and binding on both
parties.
Disputes involving    8.3    Disputes arising between the Farner Organization and third parties are

and Project         Where an amicable settlement cannot be found, the Project shall advise the

receipt of such a notice, the Deputy Commissioner shall within a period of 60
days undertake an independent investigation and decide on the matter. The
decision of the Deputy Commissioner shall be final and binding on both parties.

ANNEX 7
PAKISTAN
BALOCHISTAN COMMUNITY IRRIGATION AND AGRICULTURE PROJECT
Supervision Plan and Key Indicators
Bank Supervision Input into Key Activities
1.           The staff input indicated in the attached Table "Bank Supervision Input into Key
Activities" is in addition to the regular supervision needs for the review of progress reports, procurement
actions, correspondence and so on. For this project, staff estimate the annual regular supervision input
as 4 staff weeks (sw) in the projects first year, 3 sw in the second, and 2 sw for the remaining period.
Borrowers Contribution to Supervision
2.           Beginning with the semester ending December 31, 1995, BID's PMU will submit six
monthly progress reports not later than end-February and end-August each year during project
implementation. PMU will be responsible for ensuring that all project components are appropriately
monitored, with suitable key indicators selected and regularly measured to give an idea of the project's
effects and success in meeting its objectives.
3.           The project steering committee would meet at least twice yearly to approve scheme
selection, designs, workplans and budgets. It would also regularly review implementation.
4.           PMU will be responsible for coordinating and arranging supervision mission itineraries
and wrap-up meetings. PMU, DOA, and when appropriate NGO, representatives will accompany IDA
staff during supervision missions. The Additional Chief Secretary, P&D, will normally chair the wrap-up
meeting. PMU will also arrange, in association with IDA the two project interim evaluation missions, and
will prepare GOBal's input into the Project Implementation Completion Report. The project would
provide PMU 20 sm of locally recruited consultants to assist with supervision and the interim evaluations.
5.           To facilitate project start-up, PMU and IDA would organize a project launch workshop.
Key Project Indicators
6.           Besides project inputs and outputs, key indicators of project effects or success in meeting
project objectives which PMU and supervision missions should monitor include:
(a)    Forming and Developing Sustainable FOs: (i) whether FOs holding regular meetings;
(ii) number of members at FO meetings; (iii) FO bank accounts audited and in order;
(iv) FO members up-to-date on payments into bank accounts; (v) whether FO members
are up-to-date on training programs; (vi) whether FO has constructed CAD works on
schedule; (vii) whether FO members have any major disputes, and number of disputes
taking more than three months to solve; (viii) whether new FO Ordinance has been
drafted and implemented.



- 99 -
ANNEX 7
Page 2
(b)    Develop Self-Sustaining Irrigation Systems with Increased FO Participation in
Planning and Construction: (i) number of meetings between PMU staff and FO on
main works design; (ii) whether most FO members agreed with main works design;
(iii) whether farmers have completed their small contracts and quality of work;
(iv) quality of CAD construction; (v) whether FO members have successfully completed
irrigation construction and O&M training; (vi) whether FOs are collecting agreed member
contributions for O&M; and (vii) quality of FO O&M work.
(c)   Increase Beneficiaries Incomes and Alleviate Poverty: (i) movement up or down in
agricultural yields; (ii) incremental crop or orchard areas planted; (iii) change of cropping
patterns into higher value crops; (iv) water flow in certain parts of the system before and
after works completed; (v) water losses before and after project works; and (vi) number
of days community members employed by contractor.
(d)    Redefine Governments Role in Minor Irrigation Development and Increase Recovery
of Public Expenditures on Irrigation Infrastructure: (i) whether development of BID
training strategy is on schedule; (ii) whether BID training implementation is on schedule;
(iii) quality of BID future management plans and business plan; (iv) whether BID is
implementing eight mini-irrigation schemes on schedule; (v) whether pilot O&M transfer
program is on target; and (vi) are FOs up-to-date with capital cost payments into their CD
accounts, particularly payments following satisfactory completion of main works.

ANNEX 8
PAKISTAN
BALOCHISTAN COMMUNITY IRRIGATION AND AGRICULTURE PROJECT
Project Analysis
Scope and Approach
I .          The main subject of the financial and economic analysis of the project is the performance
of the minor irrigation schemes, which represent most of the project's investments in field works and for
whose implementation the project's community and field support and engineering services will be chiefly
employed. The mini schemes overall represent about 13 percent of project investments in irrigation works,
while the pilot flood schemes represent approximately 11 percent. The mini schemes are individually small
works, whose financial and economic returns are expected to be comparable to those from minor schemes
located in the high productivity farm areas. Although the mini schemes are expected to contribute to an
increase in agricultural production, their primary role in the project is to provide training opportunities for
improving the technical skills of the Balochistan Irrigation Department. An estimate of the expected
return to this institution development has not been attempted. Regarding the flood schemes, as the benefits
from such investments have not yet been adequately tested, the pilot component is a trial and not included
in the analysis of the rest of the project. The return to the investment for gaining the additional
information has not been estimated, but an effort has been made to demonstrate by a calculation separate
from the analysis of the project the type of outcome from the trial that would warrant further consideration
of future investments in such schemes.
2.           Quantifiable Project Benefits. The project benefits of the minor schemes consist
primarily of the incremental agricultural production that would result from increased supplies of water on
existing cropped areas and from expansions of cropped areas. These benefits are derived from modelling
the production and cropping patterns of a set of representative irrigation schemes that are based on several
recently screened schemes and on schemes now prepared to detailed design.
3.           Non-Quantified/Non-Quantifiable Project Benefits. Apart from incremental agricultural
production, some other benefits might be attributed to the project. These include improvements in health
resulting from the incidental provision of potable water supplies accompanying several of the schemes,
some improvements in the status of rural women, and the multiplier effects of enhanced rural incomes and
of private and public saving (such as further improvements to road systems and other infrastructure). If
implementation proceeds well, communities should be able to build upon their experience of operating
water users' associations to undertake other community-based development initiatives. Such benefits that
might also accrue to the project are difficult to quantify and if quantifiable, might well only modestly
contribute to the overall attractiveness of the project, but they add to the acceptability of the project.
4.           Methodology and Presentation of the Analysis. There are financial and economic
versions of crop budgets, and scheme and project models of the minor irrigation scheme development.
The financial version incorporates proposed household cash and labor contributions, project provisions for
the scheme investments and household payments into a community development fund in generating net
cash flows before and after financing.



- 102-
ANNEX 8
Page 2
5.            The economic version estimates the incremental benefits of the scheme developments in
terms of production-efficiency measures of opportunity costs to the economy and uses border prices, at
March 1995 value, for major tradeable commodities derived from World Bank projections of cif or fob
values adjusted for local handling and transportation costs to the farm gate. The derivation of border
prices for major agricultural commodities was based partially on data presented in "Pakistan: Irrigation
and Drainage Sector Strategy Review" and adjusted for current prices. The commodity price projections
used were those provided in the World Bank's "Revision of Primary Commodity Price Forecasts and
Quarterly Review of Commodity Markets -- May 1994." In general, the crop and activities budgets of
the analysis use the average of prices expected to prevail over the period between 1995 and 2005.
6.            The border prices for non-tradeable commodities that constitute minor project costs were
estimated from current local prices using a Standard Conversion Factor (SCF) of 0.9. The SCF was also
used for minor costs of tradeable commodities.
7.            The conversion factor for civil works was estimated at 0.82 based on the foreign exchange,
local cost and tax content derived for the project costing.
8.            The financial wage for casual labour in rural Balochistan was priced at R 56/day, which
was considered to be a reasonable estimate of marginal value product. The economic wage was then
derived using the SCF.
9.            The analysis used 10 percent as the opportunity cost of capital.
Estimation of Agricultural Production Improvements
10.          Crop Models. In the analysis schemes broadly are of three types: those located in high
productivity farm areas, average productivity farm areas and low productivity farm areas. This typology
also corresponds to geographically high, mid- and low altitude areas in the province. For the high
productivity farm areas, the analysis used crop models for mixed orchards, intercropped vegetables,
vegetables, traditional apple orchards, semi-dwarf apple orchards, wheat, maize and fodder to represent
agricultural production. Production in average productivity farm areas is depicted with crop models of
mixed orchards, intercropped vegetables, vegetables, apples, semi-dwarf apples, wheat, pulses, coarse rice,
fodder and lucerne. Crop models of wheat, onions intercropped with cumin, vegetables and watermelon
characterize production in low productivity area.
11.          In the future without the project, the project assumed that productivity of existing
agriculture would improve over time. Generally, this was reflected in an increase in yields of from 0.5-1
percent a year over the next twenty years. In some cases, future without and future with the project yields
are close twenty or twenty-five years from now, but with the project's extension services delivered through
its field offices higher production is achieved much sooner than in the future without the project. The
following table summarizes the yield increases assumed in the analysis.

The high and mid-altitude irrigation schemes use the same high/mid-altitude crop models. Some of the
highest yield increases are expected to occur in the low altitude areas, where the marginal benefit of
additional water for severely stressed crops is considered high. The geographic location of the these
schemes, however, will also strongly determine whether communities will actually benefit from the
production potential. Note also that semi-dwarf apples start to bear fruit in the fourth year of planting,
whereas new traditional apples and mixed orchards begin to bear in year eight of planting. All yields are
mission estimates based on field observations and data collected by BMIAD. Details of the production,
yields and budgets of the crop models are presented in tables attached to the text.

ANNEX 8
Page 4
Scheme Models
12.          The analysis defines five models of minor perennial irrigation schemes:
High Altitude -- Area Expansion
Mid-Altitude -- Constant Area
Mid-Altitude -- Area Expansion
Low Altitude -- Constant Area
Low Altitude -- Area Expansion
The second and fourth scheme models involve the addition of water on existing cropped areas. Yields
improve as crops are less stressed and changes in agronomic practices are introduced through project field
services. The remaining models anticipate some expansion of cropped areas. In each of the schemes there
is some modification in cropping patterns following the introduction of additional flows of water.
13.          The command areas of the schemes are not large. For high altitude areas, the scheme
model anticipates on average an expansion in cropped area from 44 to 65 hectares. For the mid-altitude
farm areas, the average cropped area is 80 hectares. For mid-altitude -- area expansion schemes the
cropped area increases on average from 177 to 227 hectares. In low altitude farm areas the expected
cropped area is about 48 hectares for schemes that augment supplies of water; however, one can expect
schemes such as Kunara, where cropped area increases significantly from 6 to 103 hectares.
14.          Cropping Patterns. The following tables present the cropping patterns generating the
results of the scheme models in the analysis.

The analysis anticipates that the areas planted in new mixed orchards in the future with the project will
be relatively small compared to areas in new apples. Of the areas planted in new apple orchards, the
analysis presumes that no more than a quarter would be planted in the semi-dwarf variety: despite
extension efforts and the rapid returns from dwarf variety, acceptance of the crop is expected to proceed
gradually over time. Note the prominence of wheat for the mid-altitude schemes without the project.
Under the project, households are expected to increase incomes and maximise returns to the use of water
by planting more of their irrigated holdings in orchards.
15.            Scheme Financing. In the financial high and mid-altitude scheme budgets of the analysis,
households contribute 9% of the estimated capital costs of main works in cash during scheme construction.
Households also contribute 6% of the cost of the main works in unskilled labor. The number of days this
represents is calculated using the rate charged for contracted hires. This rate is estimated at Rs 78/day.
The project, however, will pay the contractor's margin to households for their days of contributed labor.
Assuming that the ordinary rural wage for unskilled labor will be about Rs 56/day, this margin is
estimated at Rs 22/day. This income to households appears in the financial budgets under the heading
"Grant including Construction Wages". Households are also expected to repay 15% of the cost of the
main works, in real terms, to BID. This appears in the budgets in the form of long-term credit that is
repaid at 12% interest over 6 years. The project would carry the remaining capital cost of the main works
of each scheme. In the scheme budgets this residual financing appears as part of a grant to households.
16.            For the households in the low altitude areas, scheme financing reflects these areas'
relatively poor socio-economic conditions. Here, the average cash contribution is expected to be 1% of
the capital cost of main works. The unskilled labor contribution is 6% of main works investment costs,
while 8% will be repaid to BID.
17.           Irrigators will be responsible for the financing, in cash and in kind, of CAD works, which
are estimated at 2.5 percent of the investment costs of main works. The operation and maintenance of
the schemes are solely the responsibility of the households. Where scheme investments enable expansions
of cropped areas, households will also be liable for the costs of developing and preparing incremental
areas of land for agriculture.



- 109 -
ANNEX 8
Page 9
18.            Supply of Household Labor. The financial budgets of the schemes assumes as a starting
point that households at present supply about 90 percent of the farm labor required for cropping. The rest
of the labor is hired at the going rural wage. It is also assumed that the availability of household labor
will be the same both for the future without and with the project, but growing at 3 percent per annum.
19.            Financial and Economic Results. As expected, given the terms of the financing of
schemes, the financial internal rates of return after financing are quite strong, but significant demands are
made on household labor not only for their portion of investment costs, but also for the construction of
CAD works and, in the cases where cropped area expands, land development. The economic rates of
return are also satisfactory. Deciduous fruit production contributes substantially to the performance of the
high and average productivity farm schemes. The performance of low altitude schemes relies on the
production of vegetables. The returns to the second low altitude farm stem from a significant expansion
of cropped area with the project. The following table summarizes the results. Detailed budgets are
included in SAR working paper "Project Costing and Analysis, June 1994.
Table 7: Financial and Economic Rates of Return by

20.            Impact on Farm Incomes. Average farm incomes from irrigated agriculture are expected
to increase by about Rs I 1,000 to Rs 20,000 in the short run and by Rs 11,000 to 65,000 in the long run,
measured in March 1995 prices. The "short run" refers to years eight to ten in a scheme's development:
just after households have finished paying their cash contributions into their village development fund.
The "long run" is 17 to 20 years into a scheme's development. The greatest change in the long run is
expected occur for mid-altitude schemes, where the dominant cropping pattern shifts from wheat
production to horticulture. In the short run, however, the largest gains in incomes should be seen among
the low altitude schemes specializing in off-season vegetable production.
21.            The estimates of farm income were based on the following household holdings of irrigated
land. Sizes vary by scheme and whether additional land is brought under command through the project.

production, actual household net cash flows are likely to be somewhat less than what the figures show
here. There is not much reliable information on consumption patterns; the presumption here is that most
if not all of the irrigated areas are used for the production of cash crops.

23.            The project is not expected to have a great impact on income distribution within individual
schemes as the increases in income would be based on existing ownership of water rights. The scheme
selection criteria will endeavour to ensure that schemes with relatively inequitable water right ownership
would be excluded. Farmers on low area schemes and flood irrigation schemes would have large
proportional increases in their incomes, which, at a provincial level would improve income distribution
and provide support to disadvantaged groups in the community.

26.             Allocation of Project Costs. As the project's field services will contribute to objectives
other than the development of incremental irrigation water supplies (that is, public health, womens'
education, village mobilization for self help), the analysis estimated that perhaps 70 percent of project
costs for Community Support could be allocated to the implementation of the minor, mini and flood
schemes (the sensitivity analysis of financial and economic indicators will later show that this is not a
critical parameter). Of the project's 42 proposed schemes, the minor schemes would absorb about 70
percent of this allocation, assuming that the costs of support services are spread more or less evenly
among the schemes. Financial Base "aftercare" costs for the two years following the end of the project
period were estimated at about Rs 15,400 and Rs 7,700 from the recurrent expenditures of the project's
costings. Following the same procedure, the analysis allocated 70 percent of these costs to the irrigation
schemes and about 70 percent of this allocation to the minor schemes. Financial base costs were
converted to their economic equivalents using an average conversion factor of 0.88.



- 112 _
ANNEX 8
Page 12
27.            The analysis attributes all of the costs associated with the engineering services of the PMU
to the implementation of the irrigation schemes. Given evenly distributed costs among schemes, the minor
schemes' share of the engineering services is about 70 percent. The economic analysis converts these
costs into their border price equivalents by applying an average conversion factor of 0.88.
28.            On the basis of expenditure shares of project costs, the analysis considers that the
irrigation works can claim about 93 percent of the costs of project administration to be incurred by the
PMU. Of this portion, about 70 percent can be expected to allocated to the minor schemes. The ratio
of economic to financial base administrative costs for the economic analysis works out to about 0.83.
29.            Returns to the Project (Minor Irrigation Schemes). On the basis of the modelling
exercise the project is expected to increase the cropped area under minor irrigation in the province by less
than 1000 ha. The expectation, or target, may be considered to be about 920 ha over the 5 full years of
the project's implementation. Of this area, about 830 ha would be planted in newly established deciduous
fruit orchards: 150 ha of mixed orchards, about 510 ha of traditional apple varieties and about 170 ha of
semi-dwarf apples. The remaining incremental irrigated areas would be planted in arable food and fodder
crops. Together with existing irrigated areas, on which some changes in cropping patterns are expected
to combine with yield improvements, the project at full development should to lead to net production
increases on the order of 550 tons of fodder, 6,450 tons of arable crops, 3,270 tons of mixed orchard fruits
and 14,200 tons of apples. Agricultural production from the development of the mini and flood schemes
may increase these estimates by about 20 percent.
30.            The economic internal rate of return for the development of the minor schemes is
estimated at 14 percent. For an irrigation project, this might not appear to be a particularly strong result,
but it is acceptable given the high costs of working with remote and scattered communities in Balochistan,
and it appears to be robust.
31.            Sensitivity Analysis. The economic rates of return are moderately sensitive to increases
in scheme investments or project costs or to decreases in expected incremental production benefits. The
following table shows that if increases in costs and decreases in benefits fall within a band of 20 percent,
the project would still likely be acceptable.
Table 12: Summary Sensitivity Analysis

32.           The key issue in the sustainability of the development of the schemes is the role and
perfornance of the farmer organizations. If a farmer organization were to operate effectively or fail, the
implementation of its scheme would likely collapse or deteriorate rapidly for lack of proper routine
maintenance. In high altitude areas, the economic consequences of this occurring might provide sufficient
cohesion within groups to ensure that schemes do work. In other areas where the opportunity cost of
failure might not be as high, there might not be as much confidence in the stability of groups. Due to
importance of the organizational aspects of implementing and maintaining the irrigation works, village
support services are a prominent component of the project.
33.           Scheme location and distribution are critical to the economic justification of the project.
Potentially, there may several low altitude areas where cropped area can be expanded, but per hectare,
returns are higher for higher altitudes. If a larger proportion of low altitude schemes are built, the project
might be much less economically desirable. The size and proportion of small schemes to large ones also
has an expected bearing on the overall economic performance of the project.
34.           The selection of suitable flood schemes is particularly difficult due to the complex
sociology of the areas. Also, in these areas there are particular difficulties in estimating water flow and
the market linkages, if not already present on a rudimentary scale, may be slow to develop. The return
on the investment in the low altitude and flood scheme areas can be rather respectable since production
in these areas would be improving from a low base. The success of the schemes, however, would depend
on the ability of the project to provide the required extension assistance as needed and the willingness of
farmers to accept the improvements afforded by available technologies.
35.           The geographic spread of the schemes potentially under consideration may complicate
project management and add to costs. To some extent, however, these difficulties have been anticipated;
the establishment of several field offices under the project should help mitigate logistical difficulties.
36.           The performance of contractors, which was a major constraint to the efficient, early
implementation of the BMIAD, could also be a cause for concern. Strict post qualification and selection
procedures that have been established in the later stages of BMIAD, along with rigorous monitoring have
resulted in a small, but adequate pool of reasonably capable contractors. The risk of poor contractor
performance under the present project still exists, but is considered now to have been much reduced.
] The following section draws from "Balochistan Minor Irrigation and Agricultural Development Project - Phase II,
Project Preparation Report," Annex XXII, June 1993.

ANNEX 9
PAKISTAN
BALOCEHISTAN COMMUNITY IRRIGATION AND AGRICULTURE PROJECT
Project Implementation Plan
A. The Project
l.           The project objectives are:
(a)   redefine government's role in minor irrigation development and provide assistance to
facilitate change;
(b)    develop sustainable farmer organizations (FOs) which, amongst other things, can
efficiently operate and maintain minor irrigation systems;
(c)    develop self-sustaining irrigation systems, and increase FO participation in their planning
and construction;
(d)   increase beneficiaries' incomes and alleviate poverty; and
(e)   increase the recovery of public expenditures on irrigation infrastructure from
beneficiaries.
2.           The project components are:
Community Development and Support
(a)   forming and developing FOs by providing technical assistance, data collection, guidance,
legislation and training;
(b)    providing engineering, agricultural and other services to project beneficiaries;
Infrastructure
(c)    designing, constructing and/or rehabilitating some 30 minor irrigation schemes, a pilot
program of four flood irrigation schemes and, where possible, associated potable water
supplies;
Project Management
(d)   supporting a project management unit (PMU) within BID to implement the project by
providing staff, technical assistance, training, equipment, vehicles, office space and
operating funds. The PMU would include a community support group (CSG);
(e)    monitoring and evaluating project implementation through providing staff, technical
assistance, vehicles, equipment, office space and operating funds;



- 118 -
ANNEX 9
Page 2
Agency Strengthening
(f)    strengthening BID's capabilities to interact with local communities and assist develop
community planned and implemented minor irrigation by providing technical assistance
and training, including implementing some eight mini irrigation schemes as part of in-
service training; and
(g)    developing a pilot program to investigate how BID might generally transfer O&M
responsibilities to FOs of all minor irrigation schemes outside this project or BMIAD.
3.           Cost Estimates. GOBal would fully implement the project over seven years. The IDA
credit would assist finance the project over the first five years. For this period the total estimated project
cost is US$38.5 million, of which US$11.9 million is the foreign exchange costs. GOBal with GON
assistance would finance all project costs (US$1.5 million) during year six and seven.
4.           Financing. IDA, GON, GOBal and local community farmers and households would
jointly finance the project. IDA would provide a credit of SDR 18.5 million (US$26.7 million), GON
a grant of NLG 8.5 million (US$5.4 million), local community farmers and households Rs 92.4 million
(US$2.6 million), of which roughly Rs 47.6 million would be in cash and Rs 44.8 million in labor, and
GOBal the remaining Rs 135.1 million (US$3.8 million). IDA's credit would finance 69% of project
costs, including 77 % of foreign exchange costs and 66% of local costs. Excluding duties and taxes, IDA
and GON together would finance 90% of project costs.
B. Implementation Arrangements
5.           General. BID, through its PMU and in association with FOs and DOA, would be
responsible for implementing the project. GOBal would establish a project steering committee to
determine overall project policy, assist with coordination and regularly review implementation. The
Additional Chief Secretary, P&D, would chair the committee which would include, among others the
Secretaries of Finance, Irrigation, Agriculture, Livestock, Health and Public Health Engineering,
Member, Board of Revenue and the Project Director. The steering committee would meet twice a year,
or more often if necessary, and would approve scheme selection, designs, workplans and budgets. PMU
would act as the committee's secretariat, and would prepare its agenda and monitor its decisions.
6.           BID's Role.  While BID's Secretary would be ultimately responsible for project
operations, PMU's Project Director would be the day-to-day project manager and PMU's three groups,
together with FOs, would plan, design and implement the project. To allow PMU undertake its
responsibilities, BID would enlarge, strengthen and reorganize it including seconding senior and other
staff to it. However, following completion of the final aftercare period (project year seven), BID would
disband PMU and bring the responsibilities for community irrigation development back into BID's regular
work programs. To prepare for this, PMU's PDS would assist BID's management to formulate and
implement a staff training strategy including management and business plans, BID circle staff, with
assistance from PMU, would implement eight mini irrigation schemes, and PMU staff would assist BID
staff plan and implement a pilot program to transfer O&M responsibilities to FOs.



- 119 -
ANNEX 9
Page 3
