rexnental Q&M Costs  :~~~~~~~~~~~~:.:U: .:  ~ ~ ~ ~ ~ ..3    .. ..
7.             PMU would liaise with and be IDA's main contact concerning project implementation,
and would monitor overall project progress. It would prepare and submit to IDA half-yearly project
reports which would update and assess imnplementation of project components during the previous six
months. PMU and IDA would agree on the report format by end-November 1995, and PMU would
forward the reports to IDA within two months from the end of each six-month period, beginning with
the semester ending December 31, 1995. PMU would ensure that all credit disbursement applications
are sent to IDA.  Within six months of credit completion, PMU  would contribute to a project
implementation completion report, reporting on all aspects of the project.
8.             DOA's Role. DOA will second six of its regular staff to PMU's CSG. These will be
agricultural officer level (AO)(BPS-17) extension staff, preferably with horticultural experience. CSG
will station them in its four field offices, and they will be an integral part of CSG's agricultural planning,
operations and extension programs. DOA field assistants (FAs) stationed in or near the irrigation scheme
areas would assist AOs implement the project's extension program. To enable FAs to be involved in
project activities, the project would provide DOA 50 motorcycles, and FAs a travel allowance when
visiting project farmers.
9.             Individual Scheme Implementation. Besides scheme  development applications already
received during project preparation, PMU will advertise the project again in the media immediately after
project commencement. PMU's PDA and CSG staff would screen and select suitable community
schemes. Selected communities must agree to project conditions, including establishing formal FOs,
opening CD and O&M accounts at a local bank and making an initial deposit into the CD account. CSG
social organizers would assist commnunities form and develop their FOs. Concurrently the Project
Director and the FO, in the presence of the Deputy Commissioner, would sign a scheme agreement which
would detail the obligations and responsibilities of both the project and FO.



- 120 -
ANNEX 9
Page 4
10.          PDS, in consultation with FO and inputs from CSG, would design scheme works.
Following FO approval of the works, and deposit of the required FO member funds in its CD account,
PMU would tender and award the main works. Following satisfactory completion of the main works,
FO would assume full O&M responsibility for the works. A two year aftercare period would then
commence during which FO, with CSG engineering staff assistance, would decide CAD works required
and construct them. Additionally, over the five to six years following completion of the main works, in
addition to earlier capital cost payments deposited into its bank accounts, FO would repay another 8 or
15% in real terms of the scheme's capital cost. FO would collect the annual repayments from its
members. These funds would be used for high priority local development priorities. Following the
successful completion of these payments, BID would transfer to FO ownership of the constructed works.
11.          Social Development Program. CSG's social development staff would consist of an
internationally recruited social scientist (24 sm), a locally recruited social scientist (40 sm), a directly
hired farmer organization specialist and two directly hired women social organizers (one senior position,
one junior position) in the field support staff at Quetta; and two male farmer (social) organizers (one
senior) and one female social organizer at each field office (total of 12 social organizers in field offices).
During scheme screening, the social organizers would explain the project concept, rights and obligations
to community leaders and members. To undertake screening studies, they would live in the community
village area for about two weeks and collect data on community population, occupations, wealth and
income distribution, water and land rights distribution, occurrence of disputes, existing cropping patterns
and yields, livestock ownership, women's tasks, health and so on. When project staff and the community
decide to develop a proposed scheme, the social organizers would assist the community establish and
operate a formal FO, and act as an interface between the community and other project and government
staff. The social organizers, in particular, would assist FO develop its financial activities concerning
scheme financing, cost recovery and O&M. They would help FO organize its members to undertake its
responsibilities under the project, for example, receiving training from CSG staff, undertaking PMU
contract work, and contracting out CAD works. They would assist FO members with dispute mediation.
Women social organizers would assist community women establish and operate WGs, provide PWS O&M
and health and hygiene training, and organize appropriate agricultural training for women. The social
organizers, along with other CSG staff, would be involved in project monitoring. The internationally
recruited social scientist, along with the senior women social organizer, would lead, plan and supervise
the team's program. At project commencement, an NGO such as BRSP would train the social organizers
(both male and female) in group formation, social assessment and other aspects of their work.
12.          Water Management Program. CSG's water management team would consist of an
internationally recruited land and water management specialist (19 sm) and, directly hired, a senior water
management engineer and two CAD design engineers, in the field support staff at Quetta; and one water
management and one farmer support engineer in each field office. During the scheme aftercare period,
the field office water management engineer would advise and assist FO members identify the CAD works
required to handle the increased discharges from the improved main works. He/she would undertake
trials and demonstrations of alternative low-cost canal lining, and improved water management and
application methods. The engineer would also train FO members in water management, and O&M of
the whole system. The farmer support engineer would assist and supervise FO members in their CAD
construction. The CAD design engineers would design the agreed CAD works, while the senior water
management engineer would assist, review and supervise the CAD design work. The land and water
management specialist would advise on and undertake on-farm research of alternative low-cost canal
lining and simple, improved water handling techniques at the canal and field levels.



- 121 -
ANNEX 9
Page 5
13.          Agricultural Development Program.  CSG's agricultural development staff would
consist of an internationally recruited agricultural extension specialist (12 sm), a locally recruited
agricultural extension specialist (36 sm), and two directly hired extension subject matter specialists (SMS)
in the field support staff at Quetta; six AOs stationed throughout the field offices; some 50 DOA FAs
located in or near scheme areas; and some 45-60 VDAs, one per FO. The internationally recruited
agricultural economist located with PDS would assist the team with advice on crop gross margins, crop
prices and marketing data, profitability of current and potential cropping patterns and other matters.
Immediately at scheme screening and detailed design the agricultural staff (particularly the extension
specialist, agricultural economist, SMS and AOs) would meet and discuss with farmers potential best use
of additional water, and possible impacts this might have on scheine design. This would, of course. be
continued during the first year of aftercare when choice of cropping pattern could strongly dictate CAD
works required. Once FO members have decided their cropping patterns, the team would advise them
on varieties, best appropriate production methods, harvesting, marketing and storage. The team would
stage field days and demonstrations, undertake appropriate on-farm research, and produce suitable
extension leaflets, videos or other material. The agricultural extension expert. along with SMSs, would
lead, plan and supervise the team's program. After scheme construction, AOs, assisted by FAs, would
regularly visit FOs at least monthly and give advice and help and arrange training concerning farmer's
priority problems. The team would encourage each FO to nominate a person from their community to
be trained to work as a first level extension agent or VDA. VDAs would be the team's main contact with
FOs, and should have a positive personality, be respected in the community and be a farmer although in
some cases a local school teacher or such person may be suitable. If possible, FO members should
remunerate VDA for his services. As stated, BRSP would give VDA their initial training, while the
project would provide considerable in-service training for AOs and other team members.
14.          Project Monitoring. Monitoring of the project would be "management oriented", that
is, designed to provide information to assist internal project management. PMU's MIS section would
monitor and analyze project activities at four levels, that is, project inputs (number of staff hired,
vehicles, material procured and so on); outputs (headworks, canals, CAD works constructed); effects or
success in meeting project objectives (use easily identifiable and measurable indicators such as crop yields
or increased orchard or other crops planted); and long-term project impacts such as increased farrner
incomes. The internationally recruited monitoring and evaluation (M&E) specialist (7 sm) would assist
MIS section, and overall project staff, design and implement appropriate monitoring systems. This would
require a meeting of all project management staff to decide what should be monitored, what indicators
used, who collects information and who receives, and to set annual work targets. MIS should monitor
and analyze long-term project impacts through case studies, not large project-wide surveys. MIS would
have a complaints officer to whom FO or community members could bring complaints concerning project
implementation, and have them dealt with in a transparent manner.
15.          IDA's Role. While IDA would supervise the project at more than average rates during
the initial year. from then on IDA would use normal supervision inputs. This higher rate of supervision
during project start-up is justified because of the project's importance in initiating GOBal and tDA's new
irrigation development strategy. During the project's first full implementation years (July 1995 to June
1996), IDA would use approximately 21 sw for all supervision activities, 16 sw in the second year, and
about 14-15 sw for each of the remaining years.
16.          To facilitate project start-up, IDA along with BID and PMU would organize and hold a
project launch workshop soon after credit signing. The workshop would familiarize implementation staff



- 122 -
ANNEX 9
Page 6
with project objectives, components and other administrative aspects. BID, DOA, IDA (with FAO
Country Program staff), BRSP and other appropriate NGOs would undertake two interim evaluations of
the project, the first no later than September 30, 1997, and the second by September 30, 1999.
C. Implementation Plan
17.          Tables 2 and 3 detail project implementation regarding institutional development. Table
2 lists the institutions the project will strengthen, and their objectives and responsibilities. Table 3 shows
the yearly implementation of this strengthening with regard to component inputs, outputs and indicators
of effects or success.
